The Weakness of the Perfection Argument for Divine Hearing and Sight
Imam Razi discusses the perfection argument for divine hearing and sight and its weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from perfection for divine attributes is not sound and why revelation is the proper basis for affirming hearing and sight for Allah.

Core Idea
Perfection arguments can be turned back by bodily attributes — only revelation secures divine hearing and sight.

Imam Razi examines a common rational argument that since hearing and seeing are perfections, Allah must possess them. He shows how this argument fails because the same logic would require Allah to have bodily perfections like walking and beauty. He then emphasizes that reliance on revelation is the correct approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Note the structure: the perfection argument presented, then refuted, then a discussion of the proper method.
2
Identify the perfection argument
Find where the argument from perfection is presented: being hearing and seeing is a perfection, so Allah must have them, otherwise a human would be more perfect.
This is a common form of argument in theology.
3
Understand the refutation
Notice how Imam Razi refutes it by applying the same logic to bodily attributes like walking and beauty. He shows that if the argument were valid, it would force anthropomorphism.
This exposes the weakness of using unqualified perfection analogies.
4
Recognize the proper method
After refuting the rational argument, Imam Razi states that the true basis for affirming hearing and sight is revelation. He also discusses why other senses are not mentioned.
Revelation takes precedence over pure reason in such matters.
5
Review the counterexamples
The passage also refutes the claim that all living beings have hearing/sight, using examples of insects and fish. This further supports that perfection is not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدالة على صحة الإجماع . فكان الرجوع فى هذه المسألة إلى التمسك بالآية أولى فالمعتمد التمسك بالآيات ولاشك أن لفظ السمع والبصر ليس حقيقة في العلم بل مجازاً فيه ، وصرف اللفظ عن الحقيقة إلى المجاز لا يجوز إلا عند المعارض وحينئذ يصير الخصم محتاجا إلى إقامة الدليل على امتناع اتصافه تعالى بالسمع والبصر
•
و من الأصحاب من قال السميع . والبصير أكمل ممن ليس بسميع ولا بصير ، والواحد منا سميع بصير فلو لم يكن الله تعالى كذلك لمكان الواحد منا أكمل من الله تعالى وهو محال وهذا ضعيف ، لأن لقائل أن يقول الماشي أكمل ممن لا يمشى والحسن الوجه أكمل من القبيح والواحد منا موصوف به ، فلو لم يكن الله تعالى موصوفا به لزم أن يكون الواحد منا أكمل من الله تعالى، فإن قلت هذا صفة كمال في الأجسام، والله تعالى ليس مجسم فلا يتصور ثبوته في حقه ، قلت فلم قلت بأن السمع والبصر ليسا من صفات الأجسام وحينئذ يعود البحث
(1) أقول : يجب أن يعنى بالفلاسفة في قوله ههنا فلاسفة الإسلام ، والحق أن وصف الله تعالى بالسمع والبصر مستفاد من النقل ، وإنما لم يوصف بالذوق والشم واللمس لأن النقل غير وارد بها ، وإذا نظر في ذلك من حيث العقل ام يوجد له وجه غير ماذكره الفلاسفة والكعبي وأبو الخمسين ، أما إثبات صفتين شبيهتين بسمع الحيوانات وبصرها بالفعل غير ممكن، والأولى أن يقال لما ورد النقل بوصفه تعالى بهما امتاز بذلك وعرفنا أنهما لا يكونا له تعالى بآلتين كما للحيوانات واعترفنا بأنا لسنا والذين على حقيقتهما ، وذلك لأن ما قالوا في هذا الباب لا يرجع بطائل أما قولهم الحى يصح اتصافه بالسمع والبصر فليس بمطرد ، لأن أكثر الهوام والسمك لاسمع لها والعقرب والخلد لا بصر لهما والديدان وكثير من الهوام لاسمع لها ولا بصر، ولو لم يمتنع اتصاف ذلك الأنواع بالسمع والبصر لما خلا جميع أشخاصها منها ، وإذا جاز أن يكون بعض فصول الأنواع مزيلا لتلك الصحة لم يبق لثبوتهما فى نوع آخروجه من جهة الصحة ، وأيضا لا يجب أن كل ما يتصف بصفة يتصف بضد تلك الصفة ، فإن الشفاف لا يتصف بالسواد ولا بغيره مما هو ضده مع أنه صحيح الاتصاف بها لكونه جسما بل كل ما لا يتصف بصفة يتصف بعدمها وليس ضد الصفة هو عدمها وإن كان الاتصاف بعدمها حاصلا عند الاتصاف بضدها من غير انعكاس ، وأيضا إن كان عدم السمع والبصر نقصا لكان عدم الشم والذوق واللمس أيضا نقصا .
وقوله: الإبصار عند الفلاسفة مشروط بالانطباع ليس كما ينبغى ، والواجب أن يقوله أو بالشعاع كما مر الكلام في ذلك وباقى كلامه ظاهر .
That which indicates the validity of consensus. So the reference in this issue to clinging to the verse is more appropriate, so the reliable [thing] is clinging to the verses. And there is no doubt that the word 'hearing' and 'sight' is not real [literal] in knowledge but metaphorical in it, and turning a word from its literal meaning to metaphor is not permissible except in the presence of a counterargument, and then the opponent becomes in need of presenting evidence for the impossibility of attributing to the Exalted [the attributes] of hearing and sight. • And among the companions there is one who said: The Hearing, the Seeing is more perfect than one who is not hearing and not seeing, and each of us is hearing and seeing. So if Allah the Exalted were not so, then each of us would be more perfect than Allah the Exalted, which is impossible. And this is weak, because it is possible for one to say: The walker is more perfect than one who does not walk, and the beautiful-faced is more perfect than the ugly, and each of us is described with that. So if Allah the Exalted were not described with it, it would follow that each of us is more perfect than Allah the Exalted. If you say: This is a perfection attribute in bodies, and Allah the Exalted is not a body, so its establishment with respect to Him is not conceivable. I say: Then why did you say that hearing and sight are not among the attributes of bodies? And then the discussion returns. (1) I say: The philosophers in this statement should be intended as the Philosophers of Islam. And the truth is that the description of Allah the Exalted with hearing and sight is derived from revelation, and He was not described with taste, smell, and touch only because revelation has not come with them. And when considering this from the rational [perspective], no aspect is found for it other than what the philosophers, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Khusain mentioned. As for establishing two attributes similar to the hearing and sight of animals in actuality, it is not possible. And the best [course] is to say: Since revelation has come describing Him with them, we differentiate by that and know that they are not for Him the Exalted with instruments as for animals, and we admit that we are not those who [know] their reality. That is because what they said in this chapter does not return with any benefit. As for their statement: 'The living being is correctly attributed with hearing and sight,' it is not universal, because most insects and fish have no hearing, the scorpion and the mole have no sight, worms and many insects have neither hearing nor sight. And if the attribution of those types with hearing and sight were not impossible, then all individuals of them would not be devoid of them. And if it is possible that some species differences remove that correctness, then there remains no [ground] for their establishment in another species from the aspect of correctness. Also, it is not necessary that everything that is attributed with an attribute is attributed with the opposite of that attribute. For the transparent is not attributed with blackness nor anything else that is its opposite, while it is correctly attributable with them because it is a body. Rather, everything that is not attributed with an attribute is attributed with its absence, and the opposite of an attribute is not its absence, although attribution with its absence occurs when attribution with its opposite [occurs] without the converse. Also, if the absence of hearing and sight were a deficiency, then the absence of smell, taste, and touch would also be a deficiency. And his statement: 'Vision according to the philosophers is conditioned by impression' is not as it should be. And the necessary [view] is to say: or by a ray, as the discussion has passed on that. And the rest of his speech is clear.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage discusses the rational argument from perfection for divine hearing and sight and refutes it. It begins by stating that reliance on revelation (the verses) is the proper method. The term 'hearing' and 'sight' in revelation are metaphorical, not literal in the sense of physical organs. Then it presents an argument from some companions: being hearing and seeing is a perfection, so Allah must be described with them, otherwise a human would be more perfect. The author (Imam Razi) immediately declares this argument weak, because the same reasoning would apply to bodily attributes like walking or beauty. The opponent can ask: walking is a perfection in bodies, but Allah is not a body, so it is not applicable. The response then asks: why do you say hearing and sight are not bodily attributes? The discussion returns. Then an editorial footnote clarifies that 'the philosophers' in context refers to Islamic philosophers. The correct method is to rely on revelation. Rational inquiry yields no other basis. The footnote then refutes the claim that all living beings can have hearing and sight by counterexamples (many creatures lack them). It also argues that lacking hearing and sight is not necessarily a deficiency, since if it were, lacking smell and taste would also be a deficiency, which is not claimed. The passage concludes with a brief comment on the different theories of vision (impression vs. ray).

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
حقيقة
مجاز
صفة كمال
نقص
هوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The perfection argument is a strong proof for divine attributes.
Correction: Imam Razi shows it is weak because it equally requires bodily attributes, which are not attributed to Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi consider the argument from perfection for divine hearing and sight weak?
A) Because hearing and sight are metaphorical, not literal
B) Because the same reasoning would apply to walking and beauty, which are bodily perfections
C) Because God cannot be described with anything from creation
D) Because the Qur'an does not mention hearing and sight

Key Takeaway
The perfection argument for divine hearing and sight is weak; the proper basis is revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that relying solely on human reasoning about what is 'perfect' can lead to false conclusions. In your life, where are you assuming that your own standard of perfection applies to Allah, rather than submitting to what He has revealed about Himself? Will you let go of that assumption today?
?
If the absence of hearing and sight is not necessarily a deficiency in other creatures, how much more should we be cautious about defining what is perfect for Allah based on our limited experience? Are you ready to admit that you do not know the reality of Allah's attributes, and to accept them as He describes, even if they don't fit your mental model?
?
Razi emphasizes that the true basis for divine attributes is revelation, not rational argument. When you seek to know Allah, do you let the Quran shape your understanding, or do you first decide what makes sense to you and then try to fit revelation into that? What will you change about how you approach learning about Allah after this lesson?

The Asha'ira's Argument for Divine Speech and the Mu'tazila's Objection
Imam Razi presents the Asha'ira's first argument and the Mu'tazila's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument for divine speech used by the Asha'ira and the key logical objections raised by the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila caught the Asha'ira in a loop: to define speech, you already need it.

Imam Razi presents one of the earliest kalām arguments for God's attribute of speech: that a living being must be capable of speech, otherwise it would have a deficiency. But the Mu'tazila immediately challenge this, arguing that the Asha'ira have not even defined what 'speech' means for God, and that their reasoning leads to circularity. This lesson walks through the argument and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage quietly, then the English translation. Notice the compressed style of kalām argumentation.
The passage includes a brief editorial footnote in parentheses.
2
Identify the Asha'ira's argument
The argument is short: Allah is living, and a living being can be described with speech. If He were not, He would have its opposite (silence), which is a deficiency, impossible for Allah. Find this in the text.
This is an argument from perfection: speech is a perfection, so its absence would be a defect.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's first objection: clarity of conception
The Mu'tazila demand a clear definition of speech. We only know speech as letters and sounds, which the Asha'ira do not attribute to God. If speech means something like 'command', then it could be identical to will.
This is a demand for a precise ma‘nā (meaning) before the argument can proceed.
4
Analyze the charge of circularity
The Mu'tazila point out that the Asha'ira distinguish command from will by saying God commands what He does not will. But this distinction already assumes God is a speaker. Using it to prove He is a speaker is circular.
The Arabic term used is دور (circularity). This is a serious logical flaw.
5
Consider the objection about deficiency
Even if we grant that God can be described with speech, why is its opposite a deficiency? In custom, deficiency is the inability to articulate letters, which is not applicable. In fact, commanding without an addressee might be the real deficiency.
This shifts the definition of deficiency, undermining the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الأصحاب على كونه تعالى متكلما بأمور . اتصافه بالكلام فلو لم يكن الله تعالى موصوفا بالكلام لكان [؟] أحدها : أنه تعالى حى والحى يصح اتصافه بضده وهو نقص وهو على الله تعالى محال . قالت المعتزلة: التصديق مسبوق بالتصور فما ماهية هذا الكلام فإن الذى نجده من أنفسنا إما هذه الحروف والأصوات أو بمثل هذه الحروف والأصوات وأنتم لا تنبتونها لله تعالى فإن قلته أعنى بالأمر طلب الفعل قلت لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الطلب هو الإرادة ، وأنتم حيث حاولتم الفرق بينه وبين الإرادة يكون قلتم الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد لكن هذا الفرق إنما يثبت بعد ثبوت كونه تعالى متكلما وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية الكلام ، فلو بينا ماهية الكلام لزم الدور ، وائن نزلنا عن هذا المقام ، لكن لم قلت إنه يصح اتصاف ذات الله تعالى به وتقريره بالوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في مسألة السمع والبصر سلمنا أنه يصح اتصافه به لكن لم قلت إن ضده نقص وآفة ، بل الذي نعده نقصا وآفة في العرف هو العجز عن التلفظ بالحروف وأما ضد المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت [؟] أنه نقص ، بل لو قيل إن ذلك المعنى هو النقص لكان أقرب، فإن ثبوت الأمر والنهى من غير حضور المخاطب سفه وهو نقص وبقية الأسئلة تقدمت (1) . (1) أقول : كلامه ظاهر والوحدات الثلاث المذكورة هى الاختلافات في معنى الحياة وامتناع اتصاف الماهية بالكلام وكون قبول الاتصاف به موقوفا على شرط ممتنع الحصول .
The companions (Asha'ira) argued for His being a speaker by [several] matters. His description with speech: for if Allah the Exalted were not described with speech, He would be [؟] One of them: that He the Exalted is living, and the living being is correctly attributed with its opposite, which is deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah the Exalted. The Mu'tazila said: Belief is preceded by conception; so what is the essence of this speech? For what we find in ourselves is either these letters and sounds or something like these letters and sounds, and you do not affirm them for Allah the Exalted. If you say 'I mean by command the request for action,' I say: why is it not permissible that this request be the will? And you, when you attempted the distinction between it and the will, you said: Allah the Exalted may command what He does not will. But this distinction is only established after the establishment of His being a speaker, and that is dependent on conceiving the essence of speech. So if we explain the essence of speech, circularity follows. And if we descend from this position, [we ask]: why did you say that the Essence of Allah the Exalted is correctly attributed with it, and its establishment by the three aspects mentioned in the issue of hearing and sight? We grant that it is correctly attributed with it, but why did you say that its opposite is deficiency and defect? Rather, what we consider deficiency and defect in custom is the inability to articulate letters. As for the opposite of the meaning you mentioned, why did you say that it is deficiency? Rather, if it were said that that meaning is the deficiency, it would be closer, because the establishment of command and prohibition without the presence of the addressee is foolishness and is deficiency. And the remaining questions have preceded (1). (1) I say: His speech is clear, and the three mentioned aspects are the differences in the meaning of life, the impossibility of the essence being described with speech, and that the possibility of being described with it is dependent on a condition whose attainment is impossible.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the Asha'ira for divine speech and the Mu'tazila's objection. The argument is: Allah is living, and a living being can be described with speech; if He were not described with speech, He would be described with its opposite (silence), which is a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila respond by first demanding a clear conception: the speech we know is letters and sounds, which you (Asha'ira) do not affirm for God. If you mean 'command' as a request for action, then that request could be identical to the divine will. You distinguish command from will by saying God may command what He does not will, but that distinction presupposes that God is a speaker, leading to circularity if we define speech first. Even if we grant the distinction, why is the opposite of speech a deficiency? In custom, deficiency is inability to articulate, which is not relevant. The opposite of your supposed meaning (speech as a command) might itself be foolishness (commanding without an addressee), which is deficiency. The editorial footnote clarifies the three aspects mentioned: differences in the meaning of life, impossibility of the essence being described with speech, and that the possibility of description is conditional upon an impossible condition.

Key Terms
الكلام
الحياة
نقص
إرادة
دور
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Asha'ira's argument from perfection (living → speech) is straightforward and the Mu'tazila's objection is merely semantic.
Correction: The Mu'tazila raise two substantive issues: (1) the meaning of 'speech' is not agreed upon, and (2) the argument suffers from circularity if we try to define speech by its effects (command) that presuppose the attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the main circularity the Mu'tazila point out in the Asha'ira's first argument?
A) The argument assumes God is living to prove He is living
B) Distinguishing command from will depends on first establishing that God is a speaker
C) The argument uses revelation to prove a rational point
D) The argument assumes that silence is a deficiency without proof

Key Takeaway
The Asha'ira's first argument for divine speech is logically challenged by the Mu'tazila on the grounds of unclear definition, circular reasoning, and a disputed notion of deficiency.

The Second Argument for Divine Speech: Specification and Refutation
Imam Razi presents the Asha'ira's second argument from the need for a specifying attribute beyond will and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Asha'ira's second argument for divine speech based on the specification of human actions and the Mu'tazila's refutation that reduces legal rulings to God's will for reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Legal rulings on actions do not prove a separate speech attribute; they reduce to God's will for reward or punishment.

Imam Razi presents the Asha'ira's second argument: just as God's actions require a preponderator (will), human actions fluctuate between rulings that require a specifier beyond will. But the Mu'tazila respond that obligation and prohibition simply reflect God's informing of His will to reward or punish. This lesson examines the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the flow from the Asha'ira argument to the Mu'tazila response and the editorial footnote.
The argument is introduced with 'وثانيها'.
2
Identify the Asha'ira argument
Find the analogy between God's actions and human actions: just as divine actions need a preponderator (will), human actions fluctuate and need a specifier beyond will, which is speech.
The key phrase: 'فاختصاصها بهذه الأحكام يستدعى مخصصا'.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila refutation
Notice that the Mu'tazila reduce obligation and prohibition to God's informing of His will to punish or reward, eliminating the need for a separate speech attribute.
The response begins with 'لانا نقول'.
4
Reflect on the footnote
Consider the editor's comment that the fluctuation of speech between prohibition and permission before specification indicates the possibility of being described with one of its meanings.
This comment reinforces that speech is contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : لما علمنا أن أفعال الله تعالى يجوز فيها التقديم والتأخير ، لاجرم أسندناها إلى مرجح وهو الإرادة فكذلك رأينا أفعال العباد مترددة بين الحظر والإباحة والندب والوجوب فاختصاصها بهذه الأحكام يستدعى مخصصا وليس ذلك هو الإرادة ، لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ، وبالعكس فلا بد من صفة أخرى وهى الكلام وهو أيضا ضعيف ، لانا نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون معنى الوجوب والحظر هو أن الله تعالى عرف المكلف أنه يريد عقاب من يترك الفعل الفلاني في الآخرة أو يريد إيصال الثواب إليه في الآخرة ، وهذا القدر مما لا حاجة إلى إثبات الكلام فيه، فإن ادعيت أمرا وراء ذلك فهو ممنوع (۲) . (۲) أقول : تردد الكلام بين الحظر والإباحة قبل التخصيص بأحدهما يدل على صحة الاتصاف بأحد معانيه
And the second of them: Since we know that the actions of Allah the Exalted are subject to precedence and delay, therefore we ascribe them to a preponderator, which is the will. Similarly, we see that the actions of servants fluctuate between prohibition, permission, recommendation, and obligation. So their specification by these rulings requires a specifier, and that is not the will, because Allah the Exalted may command what He does not will, and conversely. So another attribute is necessary, which is speech. And this is also weak, because we say: why is it not permissible that the meaning of obligation and prohibition is that Allah the Exalted informed the accountable that He wills to punish one who leaves such-and-such act in the afterlife, or wills to convey reward to him in the afterlife? And this extent does not require the establishment of speech in it. For if you claim something beyond that, it is denied (2). (2) I say: The fluctuation of speech between prohibition and permission before specification by one of them indicates the validity of being described with one of its meanings.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the Asha'ira for divine speech and its refutation. The argument: just as contingent actions of God require a preponderator (the will), human actions are subject to rulings (obligation, prohibition, etc.). These rulings are not determined by the divine will alone, because God may command what He does not will. Therefore, a separate attribute (speech) is needed to specify these rulings. The author (Imam Razi) then says: This is also weak, because we (the Asha'ira? Actually here the 'we' seems to be the Mu'tazila or the author speaking for the opposing view) say: why can't obligation and prohibition simply mean that God informed the accountable person that He wills to punish or reward for certain actions? No separate attribute of speech is needed. If you claim something beyond that, it is denied. The editorial footnote states that the fluctuation of speech between prohibition and permission before specification indicates the validity of being described with one of its meanings (i.e., the contingency itself shows that speech is a possible attribute).

Key Terms
إرادة
حظر
إباحة
ندب
وجوب
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specification of actions proves a separate attribute of speech.
Correction: The rulings can be explained by God's will to reward or punish, so no separate speech attribute is required.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila response, what can replace the attribute of speech in specifying rulings like obligation and prohibition?
A) God's knowledge of future events
B) God's will to punish or reward
C) The intrinsic nature of actions
D) Human reason

Key Takeaway
The Asha'ira's second argument for divine speech is refuted by showing that legal rulings are reducible to God's will for reward and punishment, without needing a separate attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that when God commands something, He must will it for you personally. But this lesson states that God may command what He does not will. How does that change your understanding of the commands in your life that feel difficult or impossible? Will you obey anyway, even if you aren't sure it aligns with His will for you?
?
The passage says that the meaning of obligation is that God informed you that He wills to punish you if you do not perform the act. Do you truly feel the weight of that? When you neglect an obligation, are you conscious that you are exposing yourself to a punishment that God has willed? How should this awareness transform your daily habits?

The Linguistic Dispute and Ash'ari Doctrine of Divine Speech
Razi introduces the linguistic debate and states the Ash'ari position on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic nature of the dispute over the term 'speaker' and the Ash'ari position that divine speech is a meaning subsisting in the soul, not composed of letters and sounds.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal meaning in the soul, not a sequence of sounds.

In this opening passage, al-Razi clarifies that much of the disagreement about whether Allah is a 'speaker' (mutakallim) is merely linguistic and carries no rational weight. He then states the established Ash'ari view: Allah's speech is a meaning (ma'na) in the soul, called kalam nafsi, and not the audible letters and sounds that we utter. This distinction is fundamental to understanding the subsequent proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi dismisses the purely linguistic debate as useless. Then pay attention to the Ash'ari position and the points of disagreement with the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the key claims
List the three things Razi says the Mu'tazila deny: the essence of kalam nafsi, that the divine essence is described by it, and that it is one. Understand that the disagreement is not about the existence of something called speech but about its nature.
Razi says: 'The Mu'tazila deny this essence, and if they were to acknowledge it, they would deny that the essence of the Creator is described by it, and if so, they would deny that it is one.'
3
Connect to the overall lesson
This introduction sets the stage for the four proofs that follow. It is essential to know that Ash'aris affirm a speech that is a meaning in the soul, not letters or sounds, before evaluating the proofs.
The next lessons will examine each proof in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسم المتكلم هل يقع فى اللغة لهذا المعنى أم لا ، وهذا بحث لغوى لاحظ للعقل البتة فيه والمتكلمون من الفريقين قد طولوا فيه ولا فائدة فيه . أما أصحابنا فقد اتفقوا على أن الله تعالى ليس بتكلم بالكلام الذى هو الحروف والأصوات بل زعموا أنه متكلم بكلام النفس ، والمعتزلة ينكرون هذه الماهية ، وبتقدير الاعتراف بها ينكرون انصاف ذات البارى وبتقدير ذلك ينكرون كونها واحدة ، فالحاصل أن الذى ذهبوا إليه فنحن من القائلين به إلا أنا أثبتنا أمرا آخر وهم ينازعوننا فى الماهية والوجود والقدم والوحدة فهذه مقدمة لابد من معرفتها للخائضي في هذه المسألة . احتج الأصحاب على كونه تعالى متكلما بأمور . أحدها : أنه تعالى حى والحى يصح اتصافه بالكلام فلو لم يكن الله تعالى موصوفا به لكان اتصافه بضده وهو نقص وهو على الله تعالى محال . قالت المعتزلة: التصديق مسبوق بالتصور فما ماهية هذا الكلام فإن الذى نجده من أنفسنا إما هذه الحروف والأصوات أو بمثل هذه الحروف والأصوات وأنتم لا تنبتونها لله تعالى فإن قلته أعنى بالأمر طلب الفعل قلت لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الطلب هو الإرادة ، وأنتم حيث حاولتم الفرق بينه وبين الإرادة يكون قلتم الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد لكن هذا الفرق إنما يثبت بعد ثبوت كونه تعالى متسكلما وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية الكلام ، فلو بينا ماهية السكلام لزم الدور ، وائن نزلنا عن هذا المقام ، لكن لم قلت إنه يصح اتصاف ذات الله تعالى به وتقريره بالوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في مسألة السمع والبصر سلمنا أنه يصح اتصافه به لكن لم قلت إن ضده نقص وآفة ، بل الذي نعده نقصا وآفة في العرف هو العجز عن التلفظ بالحروف وأما ضد المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت أنه نقص ، بل لو قيل إن ذلك المعنى هو النقص لكان أقرب، فإن ثبوت الأمر والنهى من غير حضور المخاطب سفه وهو نقص وبقية الأسئلة تقدمت . وثانيها : لما علمنا أن أفعال الله تعالى يجوز فيها التقديم والتأخير ، لاجرم أسندناها إلى مرجح وهو الإرادة فكذلك رأينا أفعال العباد مترددة بين الحظر والإباحة والندب والوجوب فاختصاصها بهذه الأحكام يستدعى مخصصا وليس ذلك هو الإرادة ، لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ، وبالمكس فلا بد من صفة أخرى وهى الكلام وهو أيضا ضعيف ، لانا نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون معنى الوجوب والحظر هو أن الله تعالى عرف المكلف أنه يريد عقاب من يترك الفعل الفلاني في الآخرة أو يريد إيصال الثواب إليه في الآخرة ، وهذا القدر مما لا حاجة إلى إثبات الكلام فيه، فإن ادعيت أمرا وراء ذلك فهو ممنوع . وثالثها : أن الله تعالى ملك مطاع والمطاع هو الذى له الأمر والنهي وهو ضعيف جدا ، لأنهم إن عنوا بالمطاع نفوذ قدرته ومشيئته في المخلوقات فهو مسلم وإن عنوا به أن له أمرا ونهيا فهو أول المسألة . ورابعها : إجماع المسلمين على كونه متكلما وهو ضعيف لما بينا أن الإجماع ليس إلا على اللفظ أما المعنى الذى يقول أصحابنا فهو غير مجمع علية بل لم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا ، والمستمد قوله تعالى ، وكلم الله موسى تكليماء فإن قيل : اسم الكلام موضوع في اللغة لهذه الألفاظ ، وأنتم لا تقولون بكونه تعالى موصوفا بالكلام بهذا المعنى فقد حرفتم اللفظ عن ظاهره ، وإذا كان كذاك لم يكن صرفه إلى المعنى الذي ذكرتموه أولى من فه إلى معنى آخر وهو الأمر الذي عرف الله تعالى ما يفعل بالمكلفين في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب ، ثم إن نزلنا عنه لكنه إثبات الكلام بالكلام وإثبات الشيء بنفسه باطل . الجواب : أن صرفه إلى هذا المعنى أولى لقول الشاعر : إن الكلام لفى الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا . والجواب عن الثاني : إن إثبات كلام الله تعالى بإخبار الرسول والعلم بصدق الرسول لا يتوقف على العلم بكونه متكلما ، لأنا مهما علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور المعجزة على الكاذب علمنا صدقه سواء علمنا كلام الله تعالى أو لم تعلمه فهذا منتهى القول في هذه المسألة
Whether the name 'speaker' applies in language to this meaning or not, this is a linguistic discussion with no rational consideration at all, and the speakers of both parties have lengthened it with no benefit. As for our companions, they agreed that Allah is not speaking with speech that is letters and sounds, but rather they claimed that He is a speaker with speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs). The Mu'tazila deny this essence, and if they were to acknowledge it, they would deny that the essence of the Creator is described by it, and if so, they would deny that it is one. So the conclusion is that what they went to we are among those who say it, except we have established another matter and they dispute with us regarding the essence, existence, pre-eternity, and oneness. So this is a preface necessary for those who delve into this issue. The companions argued for His being a speaker with proofs. First: He is living (hayy) and a living being is validly described with speech, so if Allah were not described with it, He would be described with its opposite, which is deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila said: affirmation is preceded by conception, so what is the essence of this speech? For what we find from ourselves is either these letters and sounds or the like of these letters and sounds, and you do not affirm them for Allah. If you say—I mean by 'command' the request for action—I say: why is it not possible that this request is will (irada)? And you, when you attempted to differentiate between it and will, you said that Allah may command what He does not will, but this difference is only established after establishing that He is a speaker, and that depends on conceiving the essence of speech. So if we [attempt to] clarify the essence of speech, circularity would arise. And even if we descend from this position, why did you say that describing Allah's essence with speech is valid? And its explanation is the three aspects mentioned in the issue of hearing and sight. [Second:] Granted that it is valid for Him to be described with it, but why did you say that its opposite is deficiency and defect? Rather, what we consider deficiency and defect in custom is inability to utter letters. As for the opposite of the meaning you mentioned, why did you say that it is deficiency? Rather, if it were said that this meaning itself is deficiency, it would be closer, because establishing command and prohibition in the absence of an addressee is foolishness (safah) and that is deficiency. And the remaining questions have preceded. Second: Since we know that Allah's actions allow advancement and delay, we refer them to a preponderator, which is will. Similarly, we see the actions of servants fluctuating between prohibition, permission, recommendation, and obligation, so their specification by these rulings requires a particularizer, and that is not will, because Allah may command what He does not will, and conversely. So there must be another attribute, which is speech. This is also weak, for we say: why is it not permissible that the meaning of obligation and prohibition is that Allah made known to the responsible person that He wills to punish the one who leaves such an act in the afterlife, or wills to grant reward to him in the afterlife? This extent has no need for establishing speech. If you claim something beyond that, it is denied. Third: Allah is a sovereign obeyed, and the obeyed is one who has command and prohibition. This is very weak, because if they mean by 'obeyed' the penetration of His power and will in creatures, it is granted; if they mean that He has command and prohibition, that is the very point of the issue. Fourth: The consensus of Muslims on His being a speaker. This is weak because we have shown that consensus is only about the word; as for the meaning that our companions hold, it is not agreed upon, rather no one holds it except our companions. And the proof is His saying: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly.' If it is said: the word 'speech' is placed in language for these utterances, and you do not affirm that Allah is described with speech in this sense, so you have distorted the word from its apparent meaning. If so, turning it to the meaning you mentioned is not more appropriate than turning it to another meaning, which is the command by which Allah made known what He will do to the responsible persons in the afterlife of reward and punishment. Then even if we descend from this, it is establishing speech by speech, and establishing a thing by itself is invalid. The answer: that turning it to this meaning is more appropriate due to the poet's saying: 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made a proof upon the heart.' And the answer to the second: that establishing Allah's speech by the report of the messenger and knowledge of the truthfulness of the messenger does not depend on knowing that He is a speaker, because once we know that it is not permissible for a miracle to appear upon a liar, we know his truthfulness whether we know Allah's speech or not. This is the end of the discussion on this issue.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation concerning the attribute of speech (kalam). Razi begins by noting that the dispute over the term 'speaker' (mutakallim) is merely linguistic and has no rational benefit. He then states the Ash'ari position: Allah is a speaker with a speech that is a meaning in the soul (kalam nafsi), not letters or sounds. The Mu'tazila deny this essence entirely. Razi presents four proofs from the Ash'ari school and then answers the objections. The first proof: Allah is living (hayy), and a living being can be described with speech; if He were not described with speech, He would be described with its opposite (silence or inability), which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila object that we do not even understand the essence of speech as a meaning in the soul; what we find is only utterances. They also question whether the opposite is truly a deficiency; rather, the inability to utter letters is deficiency, not the lack of an inner meaning. The second proof: since the actions of servants are specified with rulings like obligation and prohibition, these require a particularizer (mukhassis), and that particularizer cannot be will (irada) because God sometimes commands what He does not will; so there must be another attribute, which is speech. The Mu'tazila respond by reducing obligation and prohibition to expressions of divine will about punishment and reward, which does not require a separate attribute. The third proof: Allah is a king obeyed (malik muta'), and the obeyed is the one who commands and prohibits. Razi dismisses this as weak because if 'obeyed' means His power, it is irrelevant; if it means command and prohibition, it begs the question. The fourth proof: consensus (ijma') that Allah is a speaker. Razi notes that consensus is only on the word, not the inner meaning held by Ash'aris. The opponent then objects that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' refers to audible speech, and the Ash'aris distort its apparent meaning. Razi answers with the famous poetic line that 'speech is in the heart' to show that the term can refer to inner speech. The second objection is that using the verse to prove speech is circular; Razi answers that the proof of prophecy through miracles does not depend on prior knowledge of divine speech. The lesson ends by concluding the issue.

Key Terms
متكلم
كلام النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine speech consists of letters and sounds, like human speech.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that divine speech is an eternal meaning in the soul, not composed of letters or sounds, as Allah is not a body.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on the nature of Allah's speech?
A) It consists of letters and sounds.
B) It is a meaning subsisting in the soul (kalam nafsi).
C) It is identical to His will.
D) It is a creation of sounds in the air.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine holds that divine speech is an eternal meaning in the soul, distinct from the physical utterances of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi dismisses the linguistic debate as useless. Are there theological debates you engage in that are similarly pure semantics, distracting you from the core reality of Allah's attributes? Will you stop investing time in such debates and instead focus on what the Quran and Sunnah inform?
?
The Ash'aris affirm that Allah's speech is an eternal meaning in His soul, not letters or sounds. When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe you are reciting something that is uncreated and originated from an eternal attribute of Allah, not merely a human construction? How does that change the way you approach the Quran?
?
The Mu'tazila deny this inner speech because they cannot conceive of an attribute that is distinct from will or knowledge. Are there aspects of Allah's attributes that you struggle to accept because they don't fit your rational categories? Will you submit to revelation even when your reason cannot fully imagine it?

First Proof for Divine Speech: From Life
Razi presents the first proof for divine speech based on life and responds to initial Mu'tazila objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'ari proof that Allah must be described with speech because He is living, and the Mu'tazila objections regarding the essence of speech and the nature of deficiency.

Core Idea
A living being is validly described with speech; if Allah were silent, that would be a deficiency.

Razi presents the first of four arguments for divine speech. The proof is based on the attribute of life: since Allah is living (hayy), and a living being can validly be described with speech, not describing Him with speech would mean describing Him with its opposite, which is a deficiency. The Mu'tazila raise a series of objections: they question what 'speech' means, argue that the only deficiency they know is inability to utter letters, and assert that the lack of inner speech is not a deficiency but perhaps even a perfection. Razi's response is cut short here, referencing earlier discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the opening of the proof: 'He is living and a living being is validly described with speech, so if Allah were not described with it, He would be described with its opposite, which is deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah.'
This is a simple syllogism.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila objections
The Mu'tazila first ask about the essence of speech, since what we know is only letters and sounds. They argue that if speech is defined as a request for action, that could be will. They also point out a circularity if we try to define speech by command/prohibition without first establishing the attribute of speech.
They then challenge the validity of describing Allah with speech.
3
Understand the deficiency argument
The Mu'tazila say that the only deficiency they know is inability to utter letters, not the absence of some inner meaning. They argue that claiming a command without an addressee is foolishness (safah) and thus deficiency.
Razi only alludes to earlier responses to these questions.

الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد لكن هذا الفرق إنما يثبت بعد ثبوت كونه تعالى متسكلما وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية الكلام ، فلو بينا ماهية السكلام لزم الدور ، وائن نزلنا عن هذا المقام ، لكن لم قلت إنه يصح اتصاف ذات الله تعالى به وتقريره بالوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في مسألة السمع والبصر سلمنا أنه يصح اتصافه به لكن لم قلت إن ضده نقص وآفة ، بل الذي نعده نقصا وآفة في العرف هو العجز عن التلفظ بالحروف وأما ضد المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت أنه نقص ، بل لو قيل إن ذلك المعنى هو النقص لكان أقرب، فإن ثبوت الأمر والنهى من غير حضور المخاطب سفه وهو نقص وبقية الأسئلة تقدمت . وثانيها : لما علمنا أن أفعال الله تعالى يجوز فيها التقديم والتأخير ، لاجرم أسندناها إلى مرجح وهو الإرادة فكذلك رأينا أفعال العباد مترددة بين الحظر والإباحة والندب والوجوب فاختصاصها بهذه الأحكام يستدعى مخصصا وليس ذلك هو الإرادة ، لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ، وبالمكس فلا بد من صفة أخرى وهى الكلام وهو أيضا ضعيف ، لانا نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون معنى الوجوب والحظر هو أن الله تعالى عرف المكلف أنه يريد عقاب من يترك الفعل الفلاني في الآخرة أو يريد إيصال الثواب إليه في الآخرة ، وهذا القدر مما لا حاجة إلى إثبات الكلام فيه، فإن ادعيت أمرا وراء ذلك فهو ممنوع . وثالثها : أن الله تعالى ملك مطاع والمطاع هو الذى له الأمر والنهي وهو ضعيف جدا ، لأنهم إن عنوا بالمطاع نفوذ قدرته ومشيئته في المخلوقات فهو مسلم وإن عنوا به أن له أمرا ونهيا فهو أول المسألة . ورابعها : إجماع المسلمين على كونه متكلما وهو ضعيف لما بينا أن الإجماع ليس إلا على اللفظ أما المعنى الذى يقول أصحابنا فهو غير مجمع علية بل لم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا ، والمستمد قوله تعالى ، وكلم الله موسى تكليماء فإن قيل : اسم الكلام موضوع في اللغة لهذه الألفاظ ، وأنتم لا تقولون بكونه تعالى موصوفا بالكلام بهذا المعنى فقد حرفتم اللفظ عن ظاهره ، وإذا كان كذاك لم يكن صرفه إلى المعنى الذي ذكرتموه أولى من فه إلى معنى آخر وهو الأمر الذي عرف الله تعالى ما يفعل بالمكلفين في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب ، ثم إن نزلنا عنه لكنه إثبات الكلام بالكلام وإثبات الشيء بنفسه باطل . الجواب : أن صرفه إلى هذا المعنى أولى لقول الشاعر : إن الكلام لفى الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا . والجواب عن الثاني : إن إثبات كلام الله تعالى بإخبار الرسول والعلم بصدق الرسول لا يتوقف على العلم بكونه متكلما ، لأنا مهما علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور المعجزة على الكاذب علمنا صدقه سواء علمنا كلام الله تعالى أو لم تعلمه فهذا منتهى القول في هذه المسألة
Whether the name 'speaker' applies in language to this meaning or not, this is a linguistic discussion with no rational consideration at all, and the speakers of both parties have lengthened it with no benefit. Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation concerning the attribute of speech (kalam). Razi begins by noting that the dispute over the term 'speaker' (mutakallim) is merely linguistic and has no rational benefit. He then states the Ash'ari position: Allah is a speaker with a speech that is a meaning in the soul (kalam nafsi), not letters or sounds. The Mu'tazila deny this essence entirely. Razi presents four proofs from the Ash'ari school and then answers the objections. The first proof: Allah is living (hayy), and a living being can be described with speech; if He were not described with speech, He would be described with its opposite (silence or inability), which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila object that we do not even understand the essence of speech as a meaning in the soul; what we find is only utterances. They also question whether the opposite is truly a deficiency; rather, the inability to utter letters is deficiency, not the lack of an inner meaning. The second proof: since the actions of servants are specified with rulings like obligation and prohibition, these require a particularizer (mukhassis), and that particularizer cannot be will (irada) because God sometimes commands what He does not will; so there must be another attribute, which is speech. The Mu'tazila respond by reducing obligation and prohibition to expressions of divine will about punishment and reward, which does not require a separate attribute. The third proof: Allah is a king obeyed (malik muta'), and the obeyed is the one who commands and prohibits. Razi dismisses this as weak because if 'obeyed' means His power, it is irrelevant; if it means command and prohibition, it begs the question. The fourth proof: consensus (ijma') that Allah is a speaker. Razi notes that consensus is only on the word, not the inner meaning held by Ash'aris. The opponent then objects that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' refers to audible speech, and the Ash'aris distort its apparent meaning. Razi answers with the famous poetic line that 'speech is in the heart' to show that the term can refer to inner speech. The second objection is that using the verse to prove speech is circular; Razi answers that the proof of prophecy through miracles does not depend on prior knowledge of divine speech. The lesson ends by concluding the issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the absence of divine speech would be a deficiency akin to muteness in humans.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue that the only relevant deficiency is inability to produce sounds, which does not apply to Allah. The Ash'ari concept of deficiency here is different.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's first objection to the proof from life?
A) That Allah is not living.
B) That speech is a bodily action and cannot be attributed to Allah.
C) That we have no conception of the essence of speech apart from letters and sounds.
D) That the opposite of speech is will.

Key Takeaway
The first proof from life is met with objections about the nature of speech and whether its absence is truly a deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim Allah is alive, but do you honor Him as the Living One who speaks? When you sin, you act as if He is silent. How will you remind yourself today that His speech (the Quran) is alive and demands your response?
?
The Mu'tazila said that having command without an addressee is foolishness. Yet you often speak (command, advise) without knowing if anyone listens. Does your speech reflect wisdom and purpose, or is it idle? What will you do to ensure your words are not empty but carry weight before Allah?
?
If the opposite of speech is deficiency, what does your own silence or refusal to speak truth say about your character? In what situation have you remained silent when you should have spoken for Allah? Name one step you will take this week to break that silence.

Second Proof: Moral Rulings Need a Particularizer
Razi presents the second proof for divine speech based on the need for a particularizer of moral rulings and the Mu'tazila counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'ari proof that the moral rulings of actions require an attribute of speech distinct from will, and the Mu'tazila response that these rulings can be reduced to divine will about reward and punishment.

Core Idea
The specification of actions as obligatory or prohibited cannot be due to will alone, requiring a separate attribute of speech.

In the second proof, Razi draws an analogy: just as we attribute the temporal precedence of actions to will, we must attribute the moral rulings (obligation, prohibition, etc.) to a particularizer. This particularizer cannot be will because Allah sometimes commands what He does not will and wills what He does not command. Therefore, there must be another attribute: speech (kalam). The Mu'tazila respond by reducing these rulings to God's informing the servants of His will to punish or reward, which does not require a separate attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
Razi says: just as we attribute the timing of actions to will because of their contingency, similarly, actions of servants have moral rulings that are contingent and need a particularizer.
This is an argument from specification (takhsis).
2
Why not will?
The particularizer cannot be will because God may command what He does not will (e.g., testing) and will what He does not command (e.g., sinful actions). So a separate attribute is needed.
This distinction is crucial for Ash'ari theology.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila response
The Mu'tazila argue that obligation and prohibition are not separate; they are just God's making known that He will punish or reward certain actions. This does not require positing a new attribute beyond will and knowledge.
Razi calls this response weak in turn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسم المتكلم هل يقع فى اللغة لهذا المعنى أم لا ، وهذا بحث لغوى لاحظ للعقل البتة فيه والمتكلمون من الفريقين قد طولوا فيه ولا فائدة فيه . أما أصحابنا فقد اتفقوا على أن الله تعالى ليس بتكلم بالكلام الذى هو الحروف والأصوات بل زعموا أنه متكلم بكلام النفس ، والمعتزلة ينكرون هذه الماهية ، وبتقدير الاعتراف بها ينكرون انصاف ذات البارى وبتقدير ذلك ينكرون كونها واحدة ، فالحاصل أن الذى ذهبوا إليه فنحن من القائلين به إلا أنا أثبتنا أمرا آخر وهم ينازعوننا فى الماهية والوجود والقدم والوحدة فهذه مقدمة لابد من معرفتها للخائضي في هذه المسألة . احتج الأصحاب على كونه تعالى متكلما بأمور . أحدها : أنه تعالى حى والحى يصح اتصافه بالكلام فلو لم يكن الله تعالى موصوفا به لكان اتصافه بضده وهو نقص وهو على الله تعالى محال . قالت المعتزلة: التصديق مسبوق بالتصور فما ماهية هذا الكلام فإن الذى نجده من أنفسنا إما هذه الحروف والأصوات أو بمثل هذه الحروف والأصوات وأنتم لا تنبتونها لله تعالى فإن قلته أعنى بالأمر طلب الفعل قلت لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الطلب هو الإرادة ، وأنتم حيث حاولتم الفرق بينه وبين الإرادة يكون قلتم الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد لكن هذا الفرق إنما يثبت بعد ثبوت كونه تعالى متسكلما وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية الكلام ، فلو بينا ماهية السكلام لزم الدور ، وائن نزلنا عن هذا المقام ، لكن لم قلت إنه يصح اتصاف ذات الله تعالى به وتقريره بالوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في مسألة السمع والبصر سلمنا أنه يصح اتصافه به لكن لم قلت إن ضده نقص وآفة ، بل الذي نعده نقصا وآفة في العرف هو العجز عن التلفظ بالحروف وأما ضد المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت أنه نقص ، بل لو قيل إن ذلك المعنى هو النقص لكان أقرب، فإن ثبوت الأمر والنهى من غير حضور المخاطب سفه وهو نقص وبقية الأسئلة تقدمت . وثانيها : لما علمنا أن أفعال الله تعالى يجوز فيها التقديم والتأخير ، لاجرم أسندناها إلى مرجح وهو الإرادة فكذلك رأينا أفعال العباد مترددة بين الحظر والإباحة والندب والوجوب فاختصاصها بهذه الأحكام يستدعى مخصصا وليس ذلك هو الإرادة ، لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ، وبالمكس فلا بد من صفة أخرى وهى الكلام وهو أيضا ضعيف ، لانا نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون معنى الوجوب والحظر هو أن الله تعالى عرف المكلف أنه يريد عقاب من يترك الفعل الفلاني في الآخرة أو يريد إيصال الثواب إليه في الآخرة ، وهذا القدر مما لا حاجة إلى إثبات الكلام فيه، فإن ادعيت أمرا وراء ذلك فهو ممنوع . وثالثها : أن الله تعالى ملك مطاع والمطاع هو الذى له الأمر والنهي وهو ضعيف جدا ، لأنهم إن عنوا بالمطاع نفوذ قدرته ومشيئته في المخلوقات فهو مسلم وإن عنوا به أن له أمرا ونهيا فهو أول المسألة . ورابعها : إجماع المسلمين على كونه متكلما وهو ضعيف لما بينا أن الإجماع ليس إلا على اللفظ أما المعنى الذى يقول أصحابنا فهو غير مجمع علية بل لم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا ، والمستمد قوله تعالى ، وكلم الله موسى تكليماء فإن قيل : اسم الكلام موضوع في اللغة لهذه الألفاظ ، وأنتم لا تقولون بكونه تعالى موصوفا بالكلام بهذا المعنى فقد حرفتم اللفظ عن ظاهره ، وإذا كان كذاك لم يكن صرفه إلى المعنى الذي ذكرتموه أولى من فه إلى معنى آخر وهو الأمر الذي عرف الله تعالى ما يفعل بالمكلفين في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب ، ثم إن نزلنا عنه لكنه إثبات الكلام بالكلام وإثبات الشيء بنفسه باطل . الجواب : أن صرفه إلى هذا المعنى أولى لقول الشاعر : إن الكلام لفى الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا . والجواب عن الثاني : إن إثبات كلام الله تعالى بإخبار الرسول والعلم بصدق الرسول لا يتوقف على العلم بكونه متكلما ، لأنا مهما علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور المعجزة على الكاذب علمنا صدقه سواء علمنا كلام الله تعالى أو لم تعلمه فهذا منتهى القول في هذه المسألة
Whether the name 'speaker' applies in language to this meaning or not, this is a linguistic discussion with no rational consideration at all, and the speakers of both parties have lengthened it with no benefit. As for our companions, they agreed that Allah is not speaking with speech that is letters and sounds, but rather they claimed that He is a speaker with speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs). The Mu'tazila deny this essence, and if they were to acknowledge it, they would deny that the essence of the Creator is described by it, and if so, they would deny that it is one. So the conclusion is that what they went to we are among those who say it, except we have established another matter and they dispute with us regarding the essence, existence, pre-eternity, and oneness. So this is a preface necessary for those who delve into this issue. The companions argued for His being a speaker with proofs. First: He is living (hayy) and a living being is validly described with speech, so if Allah were not described with it, He would be described with its opposite, which is deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila said: affirmation is preceded by conception, so what is the essence of this speech? For what we find from ourselves is either these letters and sounds or the like of these letters and sounds, and you do not affirm them for Allah. If you say—I mean by 'command' the request for action—I say: why is it not possible that this request is will (irada)? And you, when you attempted to differentiate between it and will, you said that Allah may command what He does not will, but this difference is only established after establishing that He is a speaker, and that depends on conceiving the essence of speech. So if we [attempt to] clarify the essence of speech, circularity would arise. And even if we descend from this position, why did you say that describing Allah's essence with speech is valid? And its explanation is the three aspects mentioned in the issue of hearing and sight. [Second:] Granted that it is valid for Him to be described with it, but why did you say that its opposite is deficiency and defect? Rather, what we consider deficiency and defect in custom is inability to utter letters. As for the opposite of the meaning you mentioned, why did you say that it is deficiency? Rather, if it were said that this meaning itself is deficiency, it would be closer, because establishing command and prohibition in the absence of an addressee is foolishness (safah) and that is deficiency. And the remaining questions have preceded. Second: Since we know that Allah's actions allow advancement and delay, we refer them to a preponderator, which is will. Similarly, we see the actions of servants fluctuating between prohibition, permission, recommendation, and obligation, so their specification by these rulings requires a particularizer, and that is not will, because Allah may command what He does not will, and conversely. So there must be another attribute, which is speech. This is also weak, for we say: why is it not permissible that the meaning of obligation and prohibition is that Allah made known to the responsible person that He wills to punish the one who leaves such an act in the afterlife, or wills to grant reward to him in the afterlife? This extent has no need for establishing speech. If you claim something beyond that, it is denied. Third: Allah is a sovereign obeyed, and the obeyed is one who has command and prohibition. This is very weak, because if they mean by 'obeyed' the penetration of His power and will in creatures, it is granted; if they mean that He has command and prohibition, that is the very point of the issue. Fourth: The consensus of Muslims on His being a speaker. This is weak because we have shown that consensus is only about the word; as for the meaning that our companions hold, it is not agreed upon, rather no one holds it except our companions. And the proof is His saying: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly.' If it is said: the word 'speech' is placed in language for these utterances, and you do not affirm that Allah is described with speech in this sense, so you have distorted the word from its apparent meaning. If so, turning it to the meaning you mentioned is not more appropriate than turning it to another meaning, which is the command by which Allah made known what He will do to the responsible persons in the afterlife of reward and punishment. Then even if we descend from this, it is establishing speech by speech, and establishing a thing by itself is invalid. The answer: that turning it to this meaning is more appropriate due to the poet's saying: 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made a proof upon the heart.' And the answer to the second: that establishing Allah's speech by the report of the messenger and knowledge of the truthfulness of the messenger does not depend on knowing that He is a speaker, because once we know that it is not permissible for a miracle to appear upon a liar, we know his truthfulness whether we know Allah's speech or not. This is the end of the discussion on this issue.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation concerning the attribute of speech (kalam). Razi begins by noting that the dispute over the term 'speaker' (mutakallim) is merely linguistic and has no rational benefit. He then states the Ash'ari position: Allah is a speaker with a speech that is a meaning in the soul (kalam nafsi), not letters or sounds. The Mu'tazila deny this essence entirely. Razi presents four proofs from the Ash'ari school and then answers the objections. The first proof: Allah is living (hayy), and a living being can be described with speech; if He were not described with speech, He would be described with its opposite (silence or inability), which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila object that we do not even understand the essence of speech as a meaning in the soul; what we find is only utterances. They also question whether the opposite is truly a deficiency; rather, the inability to utter letters is deficiency, not the lack of an inner meaning. The second proof: since the actions of servants are specified with rulings like obligation and prohibition, these require a particularizer (mukhassis), and that particularizer cannot be will (irada) because God sometimes commands what He does not will; so there must be another attribute, which is speech. The Mu'tazila respond by reducing obligation and prohibition to expressions of divine will about punishment and reward, which does not require a separate attribute. The third proof: Allah is a king obeyed (malik muta'), and the obeyed is the one who commands and prohibits. Razi dismisses this as weak because if 'obeyed' means His power, it is irrelevant; if it means command and prohibition, it begs the question. The fourth proof: consensus (ijma') that Allah is a speaker. Razi notes that consensus is only on the word, not the inner meaning held by Ash'aris. The opponent then objects that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' refers to audible speech, and the Ash'aris distort its apparent meaning. Razi answers with the famous poetic line that 'speech is in the heart' to show that the term can refer to inner speech. The second objection is that using the verse to prove speech is circular; Razi answers that the proof of prophecy through miracles does not depend on prior knowledge of divine speech. The lesson ends by concluding the issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That obligation and prohibition are objective moral facts independent of God's will.
Correction: The Mu'tazila reduce them to God's informing of His will, thereby not requiring a separate speech attribute, while Ash'aris insist on a distinct speech that issues command and prohibition.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the second proof weak?
A) Because the Mu'tazila accept the need for a particularizer.
B) Because the Mu'tazila can reduce obligation to God's informing of His will.
C) Because the analogy with divine actions fails.
D) Because will alone can explain moral rulings.

Key Takeaway
The second proof is weak because moral rulings can be interpreted as expressions of divine will without positing a distinct speech attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah may command what He does not will, how does that affect your trust in the sincerity of His commands? Are you still driven to obey even if it seems that He might not will your obedience? What does that say about the nature of your devotion?
?
The Mu'tazila say that obligation is merely God informing you that He will punish you for leaving an action. Does knowing that the consequences are directly tied to His will motivate you differently than if the obligation were an abstract rule? How does this change your fear of His punishment?
?
Razi admits this proof is weak. Does that shake your confidence in the necessity of divine speech as an attribute? Or does it push you to seek stronger proofs? In your own faith, are you satisfied with logical proofs, or do you rely on revelation despite weak arguments?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Third and Fourth Proofs: King Obeyed and Consensus
Razi presents the third proof (king obeyed) and the fourth proof (consensus) with the opponent's objection from the Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from Allah's being an obeyed king and the fourth proof from consensus, along with the opponent's objection that the Quranic verse refers to audible speech.

Core Idea
The third proof is very weak, and the fourth proof from consensus is only about the word, not the inner meaning.

Razi quickly dismisses the third proof: if 'obeyed' means His power, it is irrelevant; if it means command and prohibition, it begs the question. The fourth proof appeals to Muslim consensus that Allah is a speaker, but Razi notes that consensus is only on the word 'speaker,' not on the specific Ash'ari meaning. An opponent then objects that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' uses speech in the usual sense of audible words, and the Ash'aris have no right to distort it. Razi will answer in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Evaluate the third proof
Read the third proof: Allah is a king obeyed, and the obeyed is one who commands and prohibits. Razi says this is very weak because it is either trivial (if it means His power) or it assumes what needs to be proved.
This proof is often dismissed by Ash'ari theologians themselves.
2
Analyze the fourth proof
The fourth proof is based on the consensus (ijma') of Muslims that Allah is a speaker. Razi counters that the consensus is only about the word, not the inner meaning that Ash'aris hold.
No one except the Ash'aris holds that speech is a meaning in the soul.
3
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' indicates that God's speech is audible, like the speech of a creature. The Ash'aris distort the apparent meaning by interpreting it as inner speech.
The objection also claims that such interpretation is arbitrary and leads to circularity.

اسم المتكلم هل يقع فى اللغة لهذا المعنى أم لا ، وهذا بحث لغوى لاحظ للعقل البتة فيه والمتكلمون من الفريقين قد طولوا فيه ولا فائدة فيه . أما أصحابنا فقد اتفقوا على أن الله تعالى ليس بتكلم بالكلام الذى هو الحروف والأصوات بل زعموا أنه متكلم بكلام النفس ، والمعتزلة ينكرون هذه الماهية ، وبتقدير الاعتراف بها ينكرون انصاف ذات البارى وبتقدير ذلك ينكرون كونها واحدة ، فالحاصل أن الذى ذهبوا إليه فنحن من الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد لكن هذا الفرق إنما يثبت بعد ثبوت كونه تعالى متسكلما وذلك يتوقف على تصور ماهية الكلام ، فلو بينا ماهية السكلام لزم الدور ، وائن نزلنا عن هذا المقام ، لكن لم قلت إنه يصح اتصاف ذات الله تعالى به وتقريره بالوجوه الثلاثة المذكورة في مسألة السمع والبصر سلمنا أنه يصح اتصافه به لكن لم قلت إن ضده نقص وآفة ، بل الذي نعده نقصا وآفة في العرف هو العجز عن التلفظ بالحروف وأما ضد المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت المعنى الذي ذكرته فلم قلت أنه نقص ، بل لو قيل إن ذلك المعنى هو النقص لكان أقرب، فإن ثبوت الأمر والنهى من غير حضور المخاطب سفه وهو نقص وبقية الأسئلة تقدمت . وثانيها : لما علمنا أن أفعال الله تعالى يجوز فيها التقديم والتأخير ، لاجرم أسندناها إلى مرجح وهو الإرادة فكذلك رأينا أفعال العباد مترددة بين الحظر والإباحة والندب والوجوب فاختصاصها بهذه الأحكام يستدعى مخصصا وليس ذلك هو الإرادة ، لأن الله تعالى قد يأمر بما لا يريد ، وبالمكس فلا بد من صفة أخرى وهى الكلام وهو أيضا ضعيف ، لانا نقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون معنى الوجوب والحظر هو أن الله تعالى عرف المكلف أنه يريد عقاب من يترك الفعل الفلاني في الآخرة أو يريد إيصال الثواب إليه في الآخرة ، وهذا القدر مما لا حاجة إلى إثبات الكلام فيه، فإن ادعيت أمرا وراء ذلك فهو ممنوع . وثالثها : أن الله تعالى ملك مطاع والمطاع هو الذى له الأمر والنهي وهو ضعيف جدا ، لأنهم إن عنوا بالمطاع نفوذ قدرته ومشيئته في المخلوقات فهو مسلم وإن عنوا به أن له أمرا ونهيا فهو أول المسألة . ورابعها : إجماع المسلمين على كونه متكلما وهو ضعيف لما بينا أن الإجماع ليس إلا على اللفظ أما المعنى الذى يقول أصحابنا فهو غير مجمع علية بل لم يقل به أحد إلا أصحابنا ، والمستمد قوله تعالى ، وكلم الله موسى تكليماء فإن قيل : اسم الكلام موضوع في اللغة لهذه الألفاظ ، وأنتم لا تقولون بكونه تعالى موصوفا بالكلام بهذا المعنى فقد حرفتم اللفظ عن ظاهره ، وإذا كان كذاك لم يكن صرفه إلى المعنى الذي ذكرتموه أولى من فه إلى معنى آخر وهو الأمر الذي عرف الله تعالى ما يفعل بالمكلفين في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب ، ثم إن نزلنا عنه لكنه إثبات الكلام بالكلام وإثبات الشيء بنفسه باطل . الجواب : أن صرفه إلى هذا المعنى أولى لقول الشاعر : إن الكلام لفى الفؤاد وإنما جعل اللسان على الفؤاد دليلا . والجواب عن الثاني : إن إثبات كلام الله تعالى بإخبار الرسول والعلم بصدق الرسول لا يتوقف على العلم بكونه متكلما ، لأنا مهما علمنا أنه لا يجوز ظهور المعجزة على الكاذب علمنا صدقه سواء علمنا كلام الله تعالى أو لم تعلمه فهذا منتهى القول في هذه المسألة
Whether the name 'speaker' applies in language to this meaning or not, this is a linguistic discussion with no rational consideration at all, and the speakers of both parties have lengthened it with no benefit. As for our companions, they agreed that Allah is not speaking with speech that is letters and sounds, but rather they claimed that He is a speaker with speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs). The Mu'tazila deny this essence, and if they were to acknowledge it, they would deny that the essence of the Creator is described by it, and if so, they would deny that it is one. So the conclusion is that what they went to we are among those who say it, except we have established another matter and they dispute with us regarding the essence, existence, pre-eternity, and oneness. So this is a preface necessary for those who delve into this issue. The companions argued for His being a speaker with proofs. First: He is living (hayy) and a living being is validly described with speech, so if Allah were not described with it, He would be described with its opposite, which is deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila said: affirmation is preceded by conception, so what is the essence of this speech? For what we find from ourselves is either these letters and sounds or the like of these letters and sounds, and you do not affirm them for Allah. If you say—I mean by 'command' the request for action—I say: why is it not possible that this request is will (irada)? And you, when you attempted to differentiate between it and will, you said that Allah may command what He does not will, but this difference is only established after establishing that He is a speaker, and that depends on conceiving the essence of speech. So if we [attempt to] clarify the essence of speech, circularity would arise. And even if we descend from this position, why did you say that describing Allah's essence with speech is valid? And its explanation is the three aspects mentioned in the issue of hearing and sight. [Second:] Granted that it is valid for Him to be described with it, but why did you say that its opposite is deficiency and defect? Rather, what we consider deficiency and defect in custom is inability to utter letters. As for the opposite of the meaning you mentioned, why did you say that it is deficiency? Rather, if it were said that this meaning itself is deficiency, it would be closer, because establishing command and prohibition in the absence of an addressee is foolishness (safah) and that is deficiency. And the remaining questions have preceded. Second: Since we know that Allah's actions allow advancement and delay, we refer them to a preponderator, which is will. Similarly, we see the actions of servants fluctuating between prohibition, permission, recommendation, and obligation, so their specification by these rulings requires a particularizer, and that is not will, because Allah may command what He does not will, and conversely. So there must be another attribute, which is speech. This is also weak, for we say: why is it not permissible that the meaning of obligation and prohibition is that Allah made known to the responsible person that He wills to punish the one who leaves such an act in the afterlife, or wills to grant reward to him in the afterlife? This extent has no need for establishing speech. If you claim something beyond that, it is denied. Third: Allah is a sovereign obeyed, and the obeyed is one who has command and prohibition. This is very weak, because if they mean by 'obeyed' the penetration of His power and will in creatures, it is granted; if they mean that He has command and prohibition, that is the very point of the issue. Fourth: The consensus of Muslims on His being a speaker. This is weak because we have shown that consensus is only about the word; as for the meaning that our companions hold, it is not agreed upon, rather no one holds it except our companions. And the proof is His saying: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly.' If it is said: the word 'speech' is placed in language for these utterances, and you do not affirm that Allah is described with speech in this sense, so you have distorted the word from its apparent meaning. If so, turning it to the meaning you mentioned is not more appropriate than turning it to another meaning, which is the command by which Allah made known what He will do to the responsible persons in the afterlife of reward and punishment. Then even if we descend from this, it is establishing speech by speech, and establishing a thing by itself is invalid. The answer: that turning it to this meaning is more appropriate due to the poet's saying: 'Speech is in the heart, and the tongue is made a proof upon the heart.' And the answer to the second: that establishing Allah's speech by the report of the messenger and knowledge of the truthfulness of the messenger does not depend on knowing that He is a speaker, because once we know that it is not permissible for a miracle to appear upon a liar, we know his truthfulness whether we know Allah's speech or not. This is the end of the discussion on this issue.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and refutation concerning the attribute of speech (kalam). Razi begins by noting that the dispute over the term 'speaker' (mutakallim) is merely linguistic and has no rational benefit. He then states the Ash'ari position: Allah is a speaker with a speech that is a meaning in the soul (kalam nafsi), not letters or sounds. The Mu'tazila deny this essence entirely. Razi presents four proofs from the Ash'ari school and then answers the objections. The first proof: Allah is living (hayy), and a living being can be described with speech; if He were not described with speech, He would be described with its opposite (silence or inability), which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Allah. The Mu'tazila object that we do not even understand the essence of speech as a meaning in the soul; what we find is only utterances. They also question whether the opposite is truly a deficiency; rather, the inability to utter letters is deficiency, not the lack of an inner meaning. The second proof: since the actions of servants are specified with rulings like obligation and prohibition, these require a particularizer (mukhassis), and that particularizer cannot be will (irada) because God sometimes commands what He does not will; so there must be another attribute, which is speech. The Mu'tazila respond by reducing obligation and prohibition to expressions of divine will about punishment and reward, which does not require a separate attribute. The third proof: Allah is a king obeyed (malik muta'), and the obeyed is the one who commands and prohibits. Razi dismisses this as weak because if 'obeyed' means His power, it is irrelevant; if it means command and prohibition, it begs the question. The fourth proof: consensus (ijma') that Allah is a speaker. Razi notes that consensus is only on the word, not the inner meaning held by Ash'aris. The opponent then objects that the Quranic verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' refers to audible speech, and the Ash'aris distort its apparent meaning. Razi answers with the famous poetic line that 'speech is in the heart' to show that the term can refer to inner speech. The second objection is that using the verse to prove speech is circular; Razi answers that the proof of prophecy through miracles does not depend on prior knowledge of divine speech. The lesson ends by concluding the issue.

Key Terms
المطاع
الإجماع
الحرف
الصوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the consensus of Muslims proves the Ash'ari notion of speech.
Correction: Consensus is only on the term, not the inner meaning. Also, the Quranic verse does not by itself prove kalam nafsi.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the fourth proof (consensus) weak?
A) Because there is no consensus on the existence of speech.
B) Because consensus is only on the word 'speaker,' not the inner meaning.
C) Because the Mu'tazila do not accept consensus as a proof.
D) Because consensus is not a valid source of theology.

Key Takeaway
The third proof is circular, and the fourth proof only establishes the word, not the inner meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read Razi dismiss two proofs as 'very weak' and 'weak.' Are you willing to apply the same ruthless intellectual honesty to the arguments you rely on to justify your own beliefs—or do you cherish them because they feel safe, even if they are circular?
?
The opponent accuses the Ash'aris of distorting the apparent meaning of the Quran to fit their doctrine. Have you ever twisted a text or a tradition to avoid a truth that would demand change from you? Will you let this lesson confront you about that today?
?
Razi shows that consensus only covers the word, not the deep meaning. Whose approval are you seeking when you say 'all Muslims agree'—and does that keep you from thinking more carefully about what you actually believe?
