Proof of God's Knowledge of All Things
Razi presents a proof for God's universal knowledge and anticipates an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that God knows all things based on the impossibility of a specifying cause for a necessary existent, and the objection raised against this proof.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge were limited, a specifying cause would be needed, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Imam Razi presents the mainstream Muslim doctrine that God knows all things. He offers a concise logical argument rooted in the nature of a living being and the impossibility of arbitrary specification in the eternal. However, he also records a skeptical objection challenging the certainty of this proof. This lesson walks through both the argument and the challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Razi first states the doctrine, then gives the proof, and finally introduces an objection.
2
Identify the key premises of the proof
The proof has two main premises: (1) God is living, so it is possible for Him to know all things. (2) If His knowledge were limited to some things, that limitation would need a specifying cause (mukhassis). The conclusion is that such a specifying cause is impossible, so limitation is impossible.
Think about why a specifying cause would be impossible for an eternal being.
3
Understand the objection
The objector asks: how do we know the specifying cause is impossible? Is it by intuition (badihah) or by proof (dalil)? If by intuition, that is contentious; if by proof, what is the proof? The objector argues that we cannot be certain either way.
This objection challenges the certainty of the proof, not the conclusion itself.
4
Reflect on the methodology of kalam
This exchange highlights how kalam arguments rely on foundational principles (like the impossibility of pre-eternal specification without a cause) and how objections often target the epistemic status of those principles.
Consider how a mutakallim might respond to this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( مسألة : مذهب أكثر المسلمين أنه تعالى عالم بكل المعلومات خلافا للفلاسفة ولقوم من أهل الملة ) لنا أنه تعالى لكونه حيا يصح أن يكون عالما بكل المعلومات لو احتص عالميته بالبعض دون البعض لافتقر إلى مخصص وهو محال (۲) أقول لقائل أن يقول : أنت بالبديهة عرفت أن المخصص ههنا محال أم بالدليل ، فإن قلت البديهة فقد كابرت ، وإن قلت بالدليل فأين الدليل ، غاية ما فى الباب أن تقول نحن ما نعرف جواز ثبوت المخصص أو امتناعه .
(Issue: The doctrine of most Muslims is that God Almighty knows all things, contrary to the philosophers and a group from the religious community.) For us: Since He is living, it is possible that He knows all things. If His knowing were confined to some things and not others, He would need a specifying cause, which is impossible. (2) I say: A questioner could say: Did you know by intuition that the specifying cause here is impossible, or by proof? If you say intuition, you have disputed; if you say proof, where is the proof? At most you can say: We do not know the permissibility of the existence of the specifying cause or its impossibility.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage first states the mainstream Muslim position that God knows all things, opposing philosophers and some Muslims. The proof: God is living, so it is possible for Him to know all. If His knowledge were limited to some things, that limitation would require a specifying cause (mukhassis), but a specifying cause for an eternal being is impossible because it would imply pre-eternal specification without a reason. The author's marginal note (2) introduces a potential objection: how do we know that such a specifying cause is impossible? Is it by intuition or by proof? If by intuition, that is contentious; if by proof, what is the proof? The objector argues that the most we can say is we don't know whether it is possible or not. This critique challenges the certainty of the proof, but the main text's argument is presented as valid from the perspective of kalam methodology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the proof is circular because it assumes the impossibility of a specifying cause.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it relies on the principle that pre-eternal specification without a cause is impossible in a necessary being, a foundational axiom in kalam.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the argument that God knows all things?
A) Because God is living, and a living being can know all; limitation would need a specifying cause.
B) Because God is all-powerful, so He must know all.
C) Because the philosophers deny it.
D) Because revelation states it.

Key Takeaway
The argument for God's universal knowledge rests on the impossibility of arbitrary specification in an eternal being, but it faces a challenge regarding the basis for ruling out a specifying cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every single detail of your life—every hidden thought, every secret sin, every motive you try to hide? Or do you live as if some things escape His notice? Identify one area where you act as though God does not see, and commit to bringing it into the light starting today.
?
The objector in the passage challenges certainty: 'Is it intuition or proof?' When it comes to the most important matters of your faith, do you rely on easy intuitions that you've never tested, or have you wrestled with the proofs until your conviction is solid? What belief about God have you accepted without scrutiny, and what step will you take this week to examine it more deeply?
?
If you were to stand before God tomorrow and He asked you, 'Why did you live as though I did not know your heart?'—what would you say? Use this passage to remind yourself that His knowledge is complete and inescapable. Write down one specific change in your daily life that this truth should produce, and begin it now.

The Objection to God's Self-Knowledge and Its Refutation
Razi presents an objection and its refutation regarding God's self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's self-knowledge leads to circularity and how it is refuted by the analogy of human self-knowledge and the causal relationship between knowledge and difference.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge does not require a real distinction; relation suffices.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if God knows Himself, His essence must be both knowing and known, which requires a distinction that seems circular. Razi presents the objection and then refutes it using our own self-knowledge as a counterexample and by clarifying the relationship between knowledge and distinction. The discussion also includes a marginal correction about attribution to the Dahriyya vs. philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow: objection, response, correction, and final position.
Note the parenthetical (1) referring to the marginal note.
2
Identify the objection
Find the premises: God's essence as knowing differs from as known; this difference suffices for knowledge; but circularity arises because knowledge depends on difference and vice versa.
3
Analyze the response
See how Razi uses human self-knowledge as a counterexample: we know ourselves without circularity. Then he explains the correct causal order: knowledge necessitates difference, not the reverse.
The marginal note corrects the attribution of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : ذاته تعالى من حيث إنه عالم مغاير له من حيث إنه معلوم وهـذا القدر من التغاير يكفي في هذه الإضافة . قلت : صيرورة الذات عالمة ومعلومة يتوقف على قيام العلم بها وهو موقوف على المغايرة التي هي موقوفة على صيرورة الذات عالمة ومعلومة فيلزم الدور . جوابه : أنه منقوض يعلمنا بأنفسنا (١) أقول لو قال ومن الفلاسفة بدل ومن الدهرية لكان أصوب ، لأن الدهرية لا يثبتون إلها غير الدهر فضلا عن أن يكون عالما أو غير عالم ، ثم الصحيح أن المقتضى للمغايرة هو العلم وليست المغايرة بمقتضية للعلم بل هذه المغايرة لا تنفك عن العلم كما لا ينفك المعلول عن علته ، ولا يلزم الدور ، وإنما يقول من ينفى عنه تعالى هذا العلم لاستحالة التكثر هناك أما فينا فنجوزه التكثر هونا
If you say: His essence inasmuch as He is knowing is different from it inasmuch as He is known, and this amount of difference is sufficient for this relation. I say: The essence's becoming knowing and known depends on the existence of knowledge of it, which depends on the difference, which depends on the essence's becoming knowing and known, so circularity follows. Its answer: This is refuted by our knowledge of ourselves. (1) I say: If he had said 'from the philosophers' instead of 'from the Dahriyya', it would be more correct, because the Dahriyya do not affirm a god other than time, let alone whether He is knowing or not. Then the correct view is that what necessitates difference is knowledge, and difference does not necessitate knowledge; rather, this difference is inseparable from knowledge just as the effect is inseparable from its cause, and circularity does not follow. Only one who denies this knowledge of God due to the impossibility of multiplicity there [in God] says this, whereas in us multiplicity is permissible.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against God knowing Himself. The objector suggests that God's essence can be considered from two aspects—as knowing and as known—and this difference suffices for the relation of knowledge. The response argues that this leads to circularity: the essence's being knowing and known depends on the existence of knowledge, which depends on the difference, which depends on the essence's being knowing and known. The answer given is that this objection is refuted by our own self-knowledge: we know ourselves without such circularity. The marginal note (1) by the author clarifies that the objector should have attributed this view to philosophers rather than the Dahriyya, because the Dahriyya don't affirm God at all. He then states the correct position: knowledge necessitates difference (i.e., the distinction between the knowing and the known), not the reverse, so there is no circularity; the difference is inseparable from knowledge like cause and effect. Those who deny God's self-knowledge do so because they deem multiplicity in God impossible, but in us multiplicity is allowed.

Key Terms
ذاته
عالم
معلوم
تغاير
دور
الدهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge requires a real distinction in essence, leading to circularity.
Correction: The distinction is only relational, not real, and knowledge necessitates it; the effect (difference) is inseparable from the cause (knowledge).

Checkpoint
How does the response refute the charge of circularity in God's self-knowledge?
A) By denying that God knows Himself.
B) By pointing out that we know ourselves without circularity.
C) By arguing that difference necessitates knowledge.
D) By saying the objector is a Dahri.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God's self-knowledge leads to circularity fails because human self-knowledge shows it is possible, and knowledge necessitates the distinction between knower and known, not the reverse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own self-awareness, do you sense a distinction between yourself as knower and yourself as known? How does that personal experience challenge any doubts you may have about God's perfect self-knowledge?
?
If you truly believe that God knows Himself without any deficiency, what does that demand from you in terms of seeking knowledge of Him? Are you content to remain ignorant of His attributes?
?
The objection against God's self-knowledge arises from assuming that unity excludes all relation. Where in your own life do you wrongly assume that closeness to God requires eliminating all distinctions in your relationship with Him?

Objection and Answer on God’s Knowledge of Others and Multiplicity
Razi presents the argument and response regarding God's knowledge of others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge of others would entail multiplicity in His essence, and the response that multiplicity pertains only to concomitants, not the essence itself.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in attributes does not compromise divine simplicity.

This passage addresses a critical theological objection: if God knows things other than Himself, that would seem to introduce multiplicity into His simple essence. Imam al-Razi presents the argument and its rebuttal, distinguishing between the essence and its necessary concomitants. Understanding this distinction is key to preserving tawhid while affirming divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector defines knowledge as a form and a relation. Consider why this would lead to multiplicity in the divine essence.
Notice the objector's assumption that forms and relations would be in God's essence.
2
Identify the core answer
The answer states that multiplicity is in the forms and relations, which are concomitants of the essence, not the essence itself. This is a crucial distinction.
The term 'lawāzim' (concomitants) is key.
3
Examine the marginal note
The note elaborates on two problems if the forms were from the essence or from another. Understand why each is problematic.
This note reinforces the necessity of the distinction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity of essence. This preserves divine unity.
This is a classic defense of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من سلم كونه تعالى عالما بنفسه ومنع كونه عالما بغيره ، لأن العلم صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم وإضافة مخصوصة في العالم والمعلوم ، فلو علم الله تعالى الحقائق لحصلت تلك الصور وتلك الإضافات في ذاته تعالى فيحصل الكثرة في ذاته . والجواب : أن الكثرة في الصور والإضافات وهو من لوازم الذات لا نفسها (٢) أقول : حصول الصور في الذات لا يخلو من أن تكون تلك الصورة من نفس الذات ويلزم منه كون الفاعل قابلا أو يكون من غيرها وذلك يقتضى تأثر الذات من غيره ، فإن المحل يتأثر من الحال فيه : وأما كثرة الإضافات فلا يوجب كثرة الدات .
Among them is one who affirms that God knows Himself but denies that He knows others, because knowledge is a form corresponding to the known in the knower and a specific relation in the knower and the known. So if God knew realities, those forms and relations would occur in His essence, resulting in multiplicity in His essence. The answer: Multiplicity is in the forms and relations, which are concomitants of the essence, not the essence itself. (2) I say: The occurrence of forms in the essence cannot avoid either being from the essence itself, which would entail that the agent is a recipient, or from another, which would entail the essence being affected by another because a locus is affected by what inheres in it. As for multiplicity of relations, it does not necessitate multiplicity of the essence.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that although God can know Himself, He cannot know others because that would introduce multiplicity in His essence. The objector defines knowledge as a form in the knower corresponding to the known, plus a relation. If God knew external realities, multiple forms and relations would be in His essence, causing multiplicity. The answer distinguishes: the multiplicity is in the forms and relations, which are merely concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence, not the essence itself. The marginal note (2) expands: if the forms come from the essence itself, then God would be both agent and recipient; if from another, He would be affected. Both are problematic. However, multiplicity of relations alone does not imply multiplicity in the essence itself, preserving divine simplicity.

Key Terms
صورة
إضافة
كثرة
لوازم الذات
فاعل
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple attributes or relations in God would necessarily mean His essence is composite.
Correction: The passage shows that multiplicity in attributes or relations can be mere concomitants (lawāzim) that do not entail composition in the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does knowing external realities not cause multiplicity in God's essence?
A) Because forms and relations are the essence itself.
B) Because multiplicity occurs only in concomitant attributes, not in the essence.
C) Because God does not actually know anything other than Himself.
D) Because the forms are not real.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of others does not imply multiplicity in His essence; the multiplicity is in the concomitants, not the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes—His knowledge, power, mercy—do you ever feel overwhelmed, as if He is 'many things'? How does the distinction between God's essence and His concomitants help you focus your worship on the One who is simple and indivisible? Are you willing to let go of visualizing God's attributes as separate 'parts' and instead embrace them as necessary expressions of His single reality?
?
The objection assumes that knowledge requires a 'form' in the knower. Have you unconsciously imagined God's knowledge as similar to human knowledge, with mental images and processes? What would it mean for your prayer if you truly believed that God's knowledge of you does not involve any 'form' that affects Him? Would it make you feel more secure, or more distant?
?
The marginal note shows that even thinking of forms in God leads to contradictions (agent/recipient). Are there aspects of your own concept of God that, upon reflection, contain hidden contradictions? Would you be willing to abandon those mental images—even comfortable ones—to affirm the pure transcendence of God?

The Objection of Change in God's Knowledge and Its Answer
Razi presents and answers the objection that knowing changing particulars would change God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi answers the objection that God's knowledge of particulars would entail change in His essence.

Core Idea
Change in relations does not change the essence.

This lesson examines a key objection against divine knowledge of particulars: that it would require God's knowledge to change as things change, which is impossible. Razi presents the objection and then offers a sophisticated answer based on the distinction between essential change and relational change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how the objection is framed using the example of Zayd.
The objection assumes that any change in knowledge is a change in God.
2
Understand the analogy
Razi uses the analogy of God's relation to events (before, with, after) to show that relational changes do not affect the essence.
This is a key move to defend divine immutability.
3
Grasp the distinction
The answer distinguishes between change in the essence and change in relational states (ahwal idafiyya). Only the latter occurs.
This preserves God's attribute of knowledge as eternal and immutable.
4
Apply to knowledge
Knowledge of a particular is a relation between God and the known. When the known changes, only the relation changes, not the knowledge itself.
Thus, no essential change occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زعم أنه لا يعلم الجزئيات لأنه لو علم كون زيد في الدار فعند خروجه عنها إن بقى العلم الأول كان جهلا وإن لم يبق كان تغيرا . والجواب : أنك إن عنيت بالتغير وقوع التغير فى الأحوال الإضافية فلم قلت إنه محال وكذلك فإن الله تعالى كان قبلا لكل حادث ثم يصير معه ثم يصير بعده والتغير فى الإضافات لا يوجب تغيرا في الذات ، فكذا هنا كونه عالما بالمعلوم إضافة بين عليه و بين ذلك المعلوم فعند تغير المعلوم تتغير تلك الإضافة فقط (۳) أقول : القائل أن يقول إنك تقول بأن العلم صفة قديمة لا يجوز عليها التغير وههنا جعلته إضافة تتغير، وأيضا لو كان العلم إضافة بين العالم والمعلوم لامتنع العلم بالمعدومات والممتنعات ، وأيضا قد قلت الإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان، وإذاً لا يكون لعلم الله تعالى وجود فى الأعيان، وذلك أن تقول العلم بالاشتراك على عكس الصفة وعلى هذه الإضافات وحينئذ لا تكون تلك الصفة علما بالمعلومات ولا تكون هذه موجودة بزعمك، وقد قال بعض المتكلمين هربا من بعض هذه النقوض إنه تعالى لا يعلم الجزئيات منه
Among them is one who claimed that He does not know particulars because if He knew Zayd being in the house, then when Zayd leaves the house, if the first knowledge remains it would be ignorance, and if it does not remain there would be change. The answer: If you mean by change the occurrence of change in relational states, why do you say it is impossible? Similarly, God was before every event, then He becomes with it, then He becomes after it, and change in relations does not necessitate change in the essence. So likewise here: His being knowledgeable of the known is a relation between Him and that known; when the known changes, only that relation changes. (3) I say: The objector may say: You claim that knowledge is an eternal attribute that does not admit change, yet here you make it a relation that changes. Also, if knowledge were a relation between the knower and the known, knowledge of non-existents and impossible things would be impossible. Also, you have said that relations have no existence in external entities, so then God's knowledge would have no existence in external entities. That is, you say knowledge is homonymous between the attribute and these relations, and then neither would that attribute be knowledge of things nor would this [relation] exist according to you. Some theologians, fleeing from some of these refutations, have said that He does not know particulars.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection that God cannot know changing particulars because it would entail either ignorance or change in His knowledge. The answer: 'Change' here refers to relational attributes, which do not affect the essence. Just as God's relation to time ('before', 'with', 'after') changes without change in His essence, similarly knowledge as a relation to the known changes only at the level of relation. The marginal note (3) by the author anticipates further objections: first, if knowledge is eternal and immutable, it cannot be a changing relation; second, if knowledge is a relation, it cannot apply to non-existents or impossibles; third, if relations are mind-dependent, then God's knowledge would not be real. The author notes that some theologians, unable to resolve these, deny that God knows particulars. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the argument continues beyond page 178.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any change in God's knowledge would imply change in His essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the change is only in the relational aspect, not in the essence itself. God's knowledge as an attribute remains unchanged; only the relation to the changing object changes.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction in Razi's answer to the objection?
A) Between essential and accidental change
B) Between essential and relational change
C) Between knowledge and ignorance
D) Between God's knowledge and human knowledge

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of changing particulars does not entail change in His essence because the change is only in the relational aspect between God and the known.

?

How does this lesson free you to engage with changing realities without fearing loss of identity? Name one relationship you will now approach differently, trusting that change in relation does not change your essence.

Further Objections to the Relational Account of Divine Knowledge
Razi presents further objections to the relational account of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the further objections raised against the relational understanding of God's knowledge, including problems with non-existents and the reality of relations.

Core Idea
If knowledge is a relation, it cannot apply to non-existents or be real.

After presenting the answer that knowledge is a relation, Razi anticipates three further objections: first, that an eternal attribute cannot be a changing relation; second, that a relation cannot apply to non-existent or impossible objects; third, that if relations are mind-dependent, then God's knowledge would not be real. This lesson examines these objections and notes that some theologians chose to deny that God knows particulars altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal objections
Read the Arabic passage from the marginal note and its translation. Identify the three distinct objections.
These objections are presented as a response to the relational answer.
2
Understand the first objection
The objector argues that an eternal immutable attribute cannot be a changing relation. This points to a tension between the classical doctrine of God's attributes and the relational view.
3
Understand the second objection
If knowledge is a relation, it requires a real object. How can it relate to non-existents (ma'dumat) or impossibles (mumtani'at)?
This challenges the universality of divine knowledge.
4
Understand the third objection
If relations have no external existence (as per Ash'ari metaphysics), then God's knowledge as a relation would lack external reality.
This would undermine the reality of God's knowledge.
5
Note the conclusion
Some theologians, unable to resolve these objections, denied that God knows particulars. This shows the seriousness of the problem.
The passage ends abruptly, indicating the debate continues.

Objection and Answer on God's Self-Knowledge
Razi presents an objection about God's self-knowledge requiring distinction, its circularity, and the correct response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that God's self-knowledge implies circularity and how the response refutes it by distinguishing the causal relationship between knowledge and distinction.

Core Idea
Knowledge creates distinction, not the other way around — no circularity in divine self-knowledge.

Razi presents a sophisticated objection from the philosophers: if God knows Himself, that requires a distinction between knower and known, which seems to introduce multiplicity. The answer shows that this objection leads to circularity, but the correct response is that knowledge is the cause of the relative distinction, not its effect. This lesson explains the argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the premise that the objector claims being knowing and being known are different aspects sufficient for a relation without multiplicity.
2
Identify the circularity argument
Find where the rejoinder points out that knowledge and distinction are mutually dependent, leading to circularity (دور). Note the logical steps.
3
Understand the resolution
The editorial note corrects the attribution and clarifies that knowledge is the cause (مقتضى) of the distinction, not the other way. See how this breaks the circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : ذاته تعالى من حيث إنه عالم مغاير له من حيث إنه معلوم وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفي في هذه الإضافة . قلت : صيرورة الذات عالمة ومعلومة يتوقف على قيام العلم بها وهو موقوف على المغايرة التي هي موقوفة على صيرورة الذات عالمة ومعلومة فيلزم الدور . جوابه : أنه منقوض يعلمنا بأنفسنا (١) أقول لو قال ومن الفلاسفة بدل ومن الدهرية لكان أصوب، لأن الدهرية لا يثبتون إلها غير الدهر فضلا عن أن يكون عالما أو غير عالم، ثم الصحيح أن المقتضى للمغايرة هو العلم وليست المغايرة بمقتضية للعلم بل هذه المغايرة لا تنفك عن العلم كما لا ينفك المعلول عن علته، ولا يلزم الدور، وإنما يقول من ينفى عنه تعالى هذا العلم لاستحالة التكثر هناك أما فينا فنجوزه الجواز التكثر هونا
If you say: His essence, exalted, insofar as He is knowing is different from it insofar as it is known, and this amount of difference suffices for this relation. I say: The becoming of the essence as knowing and known depends on the subsistence of knowledge in it, and it depends on the difference, which depends on the becoming of the essence as knowing and known, so circularity follows. Its answer: It is refuted by our knowledge of ourselves (1). I say: If he had said 'from the philosophers' instead of 'from the materialists', it would be more correct, because the materialists do not affirm a god other than time, let alone that He be knowing or not knowing. Then the correct view is that what necessitates the difference is knowledge, not that the difference necessitates knowledge; rather, this difference is inseparable from knowledge just as the effect is inseparable from its cause. And circularity does not follow. Only he who denies this knowledge of Him, exalted, because of the impossibility of multiplicity there, while in us we permit multiplicity here.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against God's knowledge of Himself. The objector argues that for God to know Himself, there must be some distinction between the knower and the known, but such a distinction would imply multiplicity in the divine essence. The answer (from the author) points out that this leads to circularity: the knowledge depends on distinction, but distinction depends on knowledge. A further response (jawaab) says the circularity argument is invalid because it is refuted by our own self-knowledge, where we know ourselves without such circularity. The editorial note (أقول) corrects the misattribution of the objection to the materialists (دهرية) instead of the philosophers (فلاسفة), since materialists deny God outright. It then clarifies that the real relationship is that knowledge necessitates a kind of relative distinction, but not vice versa; the distinction is an inseparable concomitant of knowledge, not a precondition. Thus, no circularity occurs. The note also mentions that the objector's real reason for denying God's knowledge of others is the impossibility of multiplicity in the divine essence, while in created beings multiplicity is permissible.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
مغايرة
إضافة
دور
دهرية
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any distinction in God's essence implies multiplicity and composition.
Correction: The relative distinction (إضافة) between knower and known does not entail real multiplicity; it is a logical relation based on knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the editorial note (أقول) in response to the objection?
A) It supports the objector's view that God cannot know Himself.
B) It corrects the attribution of the objection to the materialists and explains that knowledge is the cause of the relative distinction, not vice versa.
C) It claims that the circularity argument is valid and cannot be refuted.
D) It argues that God does not know particulars.

Key Takeaway
The key point is that God's knowledge of Himself does not require a real distinction in His essence; a relative distinction based on knowledge is sufficient and does not lead to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's self-knowledge is immediate and without circularity. In contrast, your self-knowledge is often distorted by pride or self-deception. What is one area of your life where you have been dishonest with yourself, and how will you bring that truth before God today?
?
The objector thought that any distinction in God implies limitation. Do you sometimes imagine that God's knowledge of you is limited like human knowledge — forgetting that He knows you perfectly without any need for external information? How does that misconception affect your relationship with Him, and what will you do to correct it?

God's Knowledge of Others and Multiplicity in Essence
Objection and answer on knowledge causing multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God's knowledge of many things implies multiplicity in His essence is answered by distinguishing between the essence itself and its necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in knowledge does not mean multiplicity in the essence.

Some early theologians accepted that God knows Himself but denied that He knows anything else, fearing that multiple objects of knowledge would introduce multiplicity into His essence. This passage presents that objection and the sophisticated response: the forms and relations of knowledge are not the essence itself but its necessary concomitants. Understanding this distinction is key to preserving divine simplicity while affirming comprehensive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully: the objector's premise that knowledge is an image and relation, and the conclusion that multiple known objects would cause multiplicity in God's essence.
Notice that they already accept God knows Himself; the dispute is about knowledge of others.
2
Analyze the answer
Read the response: the multiplicity is in the images and relations, which are necessary concomitants (lawaazim) of the essence, not the essence itself. This is the key distinction.
A concomitant is something that necessarily accompanies the essence without being part of it.
3
Examine the elaboration
Read the editorial note: it explains why the images cannot arise from the essence itself (would make God a recipient) or from outside (would imply passivity), and clarifies that relations do not cause multiplicity in the essence.
This elaborates the answer and reinforces that the essence remains one.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the main distinction. Then consider how this reasoning preserves God's unity while allowing knowledge of many things.
This distinction is common in kalam for other attributes too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من سلم كونه تعالى عالما بنفسه ومنع كونه عالما بغيره، لأن العلم صورة مساوية للمعلوم في العالم وإضافة مخصوصة في العالم والمعلوم، فلو علم الله تعالى الحقائق لحصلت تلك الصور وتلك الإضافات في ذاته تعالى فيحصل الكثرة في ذاته والجواب : أن الكثرة في الصور والإضافات وهو من لوازم الذات لا نفسها (٢) أقول : حصول الصور في الذات لا يخلو من أن تكون تلك الصورة من نفس الذات ويلزم منه كون الفاعل قابلا أو يكون من غيرها وذلك يقتضى تأثر الذات من غيره، فإن المحل يتأثر من الحال فيه : وأما كثرة الإضافات فلا يوجب كثرة الدات
And among them are those who affirmed that He, exalted, is knowing of Himself but denied that He is knowing of others, because knowledge is an image matching the known in the knower and a specific relation in the knower and the known. So if God, exalted, knew realities, those images and those relations would occur in His essence, exalted, thus multiplicity would occur in His essence. The answer: The multiplicity is in the images and relations, and that is among the necessary concomitants of the essence, not the essence itself. (2) I say: The occurrence of images in the essence is either that image is from the essence itself, which would entail that the agent is a recipient, or it is from something else, which would entail the essence being affected by another, for the locus is affected by what inheres in it. As for the multiplicity of relations, it does not necessitate multiplicity of the essence.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: some thinkers admit God knows Himself but deny He knows anything else. Their reasoning is that knowledge is a form (image) that corresponds to the known object and exists in the knower, along with a special relation. If God knew many things, many forms and relations would exist in His essence, causing multiplicity (كثرة) in Him, which is impossible. The author's answer (الجواب) distinguishes between the essence and its necessary concomitants (لوازم): the multiplicity is in the forms and relations, which are not the essence itself but occur as concomitants. The editorial note (أقول) elaborates: if the images were from the essence itself, that would make God both agent and recipient (which is problematic); if from outside, God would be affected. The note then clarifies that multiplicity of relations does not imply multiplicity in the essence. Thus, God can know many things without His essence becoming composite.

Key Terms
علم
صورة
إضافة
كثرة
ذات
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows many distinct things, then His essence must contain many distinct parts.
Correction: The multiplicity resides in the relations and mental forms (concomitants), not in the essence itself. The essence remains one.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the passage, where does multiplicity occur when God knows multiple things?
A) In the essence of God Himself.
B) In the images and relations, which are necessary concomitants of the essence.
C) In the known objects only, not in God.
D) In the act of knowing, not in the knower.

Key Takeaway
God can know many distinct realities without His essence becoming multiple, because the multiplicity is confined to the relations and forms that are necessary concomitants, not the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's knowledge of your every thought and deed, do your insinuations (waswas) lead you to imagine a God whose essence is 'affected' or 'burdened' by that knowledge? How does this lesson expose that subtle form of anthropomorphism, and what will you do to root it out from your heart?
?
The objector assumed that if God knows many things, His unity would be compromised. In your own spiritual life, do you ever hesitate to fully trust that God knows your complexities because you fear it somehow diminishes His majesty? How does the distinction between essence and concomitant free you to run to Him without reservation?
?
If multiplicity of relations does not tarnish God's oneness, then your many changing states—sin, repentance, joy, grief—are all known to Him without affecting His perfection. Are you living as if this is true? What one concrete habit can you change today to live more transparently before the One whose essence remains untouched by your multiplicity?

The Objection of Change from Particulars
Razi presents the objection and the answer about relational change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that God's knowledge of changing particulars would imply change is answered by distinguishing between change in essence and change in relational states.

Core Idea
Change in relations does not change the essence.

A classical objection holds that if God knows changing particulars, His knowledge must either become ignorance or change. The answer introduces a crucial distinction: change in relational aspects does not affect the divine essence. This lesson unpacks that answer and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the objector's argument and the response. Note the example of Zayd in the house.
The answer begins with 'والجواب'.
2
Identify the key distinction
The answer distinguishes between change in the essence and change in relational states (الأحوال الإضافية). Grasp why this distinction resolves the objection.
Relational change is like God being 'before' and 'after' events.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider how the relation of knowledge changes with the known object without altering the knower. This is the core insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زعم أنه لا يعلم الجزئيات لأنه لو علم كون زيد في الدار فعند خروجه عنها إن بقى العلم الأول كان جهلا وإن لم يبق كان تغيرا . والجواب : أنك إن عنيت بالتغير وقوع التغير فى الأحوال الإضافية فلم قلت إنه محال وكذلك فإن الله تعالى كان قبلا لكل حادث ثم يصير معه ثم يصير بعده والتغير فى الإضافات لا يوجب تغيرا في الذات ، فكذا هنا كونه عالما بالمعلوم إضافة بين عليه و بين ذلك المعلوم فعند تغير المعلوم تتغير تلك الإضافة فقط (٣) أقول : القائل أن يقول إنك تقول بأن العلم صفة قديمة لا يجوز عليها التغير وههنا جعلته إضافة تتغير، وأيضا لو كان العلم إضافة بين العالم والمعلوم لامتنع العلم بالمعدومات والممتنعات ، وأيضا قد قلت الإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان، وإذاً لا يكون لعلم الله تعالى وجود فى الأعيان، وذلك أن تقول العلم بالاشتراك على عكس الصفة وعلى هذه الإضافات وحينئذ لا تكون تلك الصفة علما بالمعلومات ولا تكون هذه موجودة بزعمك، وقد قال بعض المتكلمين هربا من بعض هذه النقوض إنه تعالى لا يعلم الجزئيات منه
And among them are those who claimed that He does not know particulars, because if He knew that Zayd is in the house, then when he leaves it, if the first knowledge remained it would be ignorance, and if it did not remain it would be change. The answer: If you mean by change the occurrence of change in relational states, then why do you say it is impossible? Similarly, God, exalted, was before every event, then becomes with it, then becomes after it, and change in relations does not necessitate change in the essence. So here, His being knowing of the known is a relation between Him and that known; so when the known changes, that relation alone changes. (3) I say: The objector might say: You claim that knowledge is an eternal attribute upon which change is impermissible, yet here you make it a relation that changes. Also, if knowledge were a relation between the knower and the known, knowledge of non-existents and impossibles would be impossible. Also, you have said that relations have no existence in external entities, so then God's knowledge would have no existence in external entities. So you must say that knowledge is used equivocally, opposite to the attribute, and concerning these relations; then that attribute would not be knowledge of known things, nor would these exist according to your claim. And some theologians have said, fleeing from some of these refutations, that He, exalted, does not know particulars from them.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection concerning divine knowledge of changing particulars. The objector argues: if God knew that Zayd is in the house, then when Zayd leaves, either the knowledge remains (becoming ignorance) or changes (implying change in God). The answer (الجواب) distinguishes between change in the essence and change in relational states (الأحوال الإضافية). Change in relations does not entail change in the essence; God's relation to events changes as events occur, but His essence remains unchanged. The editorial note (أقول) raises further objections: first, if knowledge is an eternal unchangeable attribute, it cannot be a changing relation. Second, if knowledge is a relation, knowledge of non-existents and impossibles becomes impossible. Third, if relations have no external existence, God's knowledge would have no external reality. The note suggests that 'knowledge' might be used equivocally (بالاشتراك) between the attribute and the relation, but this leads to problems. Some theologians evade these issues by denying God's knowledge of particulars entirely.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in God's knowledge would imply change in His essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that change can occur in relational aspects without affecting the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why does God's knowledge of changing particulars not imply change in His essence?
A) God's knowledge is not really about particulars.
B) Change occurs only in the relational aspect of knowledge, not in the essence.
C) God's knowledge is eternal and immutable, so it never changes.
D) The objector's example of Zayd is invalid.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of changing particulars involves only a relational change, not a change in His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a sudden change in your life—a loss, a new job, a broken relationship—do you ever doubt that God still knows you completely? How does knowing that His knowledge changes only in relation, not in His essence, restore your trust in His constant awareness of your situation? Commit to reminding yourself of this truth the next time you feel forgotten.
?
Think of a sin you committed and then repented from. In that moment, did you imagine God's knowledge of you had somehow 'updated' as if He hadn't known before? How does the distinction between essential and relational change free you from the fear that God’s perfection is diminished by your imperfections? Today, thank Him for knowing you fully without changing.
?
The objection about Zayd suggests that if God's knowledge changes, He is imperfect. Can you identify a time when you treated God as if He couldn't handle the changing details of your life—by not praying, by hiding, by worrying? How does this lesson call you to live more openly before the One whose knowledge relates to every detail yet whose essence remains unchanging?

Further Objections to Knowledge as Relation
Razi lists further objections to the relational view of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against understanding divine knowledge as a relation: the problem of change for an eternal attribute, knowledge of non-existents, and the lack of external existence.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge as a relation creates new theological puzzles.

After presenting the relational answer, the text raises further objections: if knowledge is an eternal attribute it cannot change, if it is a relation then non-existents cannot be known, and relations have no external reality. This lesson examines these challenges and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional objections
Read the passage starting from 'أقول: القائل أن يقول'. Notice the three distinct objections presented.
Each objection begins with 'وأيضا' or similar.
2
Analyze each objection
Break down each objection: (1) eternal attribute vs. changing relation, (2) impossibility of knowing non-existents, (3) relations have no external existence.
Consider how each targets the relational definition.
3
Consider the concluding remark
The passage ends with some theologians denying knowledge of particulars to escape these problems. Reflect on why that might be considered an extreme response.

Can God Know Non-Existent Individuals?
Razi presents the first proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that God cannot know particulars before they exist because they are pure non-existence, and the refutation that we ourselves know future non-existents.

Core Idea
If non-existence prevented knowledge, we could not know tomorrow's sunrise.

Razi presents a fresh objection from those who limit God's omniscience. They argue that before a thing enters existence, it is nothing—no distinction, no reality—so it cannot be known. This strikes at the root of divine foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how the objector defines a particular before existence as 'pure non-existence' (نفي محض) and argues it cannot be known.
The objector concedes God knows essences (الماهية) but not individuals before they exist.
2
Understand the refutation
The author's answer is that we ourselves know non-existent individuals—like the sunrise tomorrow. This directly refutes the claim that non-existence makes knowledge impossible.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation preserves God's omniscience: if humans can know future particulars, God certainly can.
This is a key point in the broader debate on divine foreknowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زعم أنه لا يعلم الجزئيات إلا عند وقوعها وقبل ذلك فإنه لا يعلم إلا الماهية واحتج بوجهين الأول أن المعلوم متميز والشخص قبل وجوده نفى محض فلا يكون فى نفسه متميزا فلا يصح أن يكون معلوما الثاني أنه تعالى لو علم الأشياء قبل وقوعها فكل ما علم فهو واجب الوقوع لأن عدم وقوعه يفضي إلى انقلاب العلم جهلا وهو محال والمؤدي إلى المحال محال فعدم وقوعه محال فوقوعه واجب وحينئذ يلزم الحر، وإن لا يتمكن الحيوان من فعل أصلا بل يكون كالجماد لأن ما علم وقوعه فهو واجب وما علم عدمه فهو ممتنع والجواب عن الأول أنه منقوض يعلمنا بالمعدومات الشخصية قبل وقوعها كعلمنا بطلوع الشمس غدا وعن الثاني بالتزام ما أن علم الله تعالى وقوعه فهو واجب الوقوع (۱) أقول : يريد و بمنهم، من المخالفين والكلام في صحة كون المعدوم معلوما قد مر، وأما التزام أن ما علم الله تعالى وقوعه فهو واجب الوقوع أنه واجب الصدور عن علمه بأن يكون علمه موجدا له كان متعرضا لعلمه تعالى بذاته وبالمعدومات، وإن إرادته واجب المطابقة لعلمه فهو صحيح، ولا يلزم منه جبر، لأنه عالم بما سيوجده وليس بمجهول وذلك لأن هذا الوجوب وجوب لاحق لا سابق والمعدومات مطابقة لعلمه بها، لأنه تعالى يعلمها معدومة، وهي كذلك بمعنى أن المتصور منها ليس وجود في الخارج
And among them are those who claimed that He does not know particulars except at their occurrence, and before that He knows only the essence, and he argued with two proofs: First, that the known is distinguished, and the individual before its existence is pure non-existence, so it is not distinguished in itself, so it cannot be known. Second, that if He, exalted, knew things before their occurrence, then everything He knows is necessary of occurrence, because its non-occurrence would lead to knowledge turning into ignorance, which is impossible, and what leads to the impossible is impossible, so its non-occurrence is impossible, so its occurrence is necessary. And then compulsion would follow, and the animal would not be able to do anything at all, but would be like inanimate matter, because what He knows to occur is necessary, and what He knows not to occur is impossible. The answer to the first: It is refuted by our knowledge of non-existent individuals before their occurrence, like our knowledge of the sunrise tomorrow. And to the second: By accepting that what God, exalted, knows to occur is necessary of occurrence. (1) I say: He means 'and among them' from the opponents. The discussion about the correctness of non-existent being known has passed. As for accepting that what God, exalted, knows to occur is necessary of occurrence, that it is necessary of emanation from His knowledge, that His knowledge is productive of it, it is an engagement with His knowledge of Himself and of non-existents. And that His will necessarily corresponds to His knowledge is correct, and compulsion does not follow from it, because He knows what He will bring into existence, and it is not unknown. And that is because this necessity is a subsequent necessity, not prior, and non-existents correspond to His knowledge of them, because He knows them as non-existent, and they are so, meaning that the conceived of them has no existence in the external world.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection: some claim God knows particulars only at the moment they occur; before that, He knows only the universal essence (الماهية). Two proofs are given: (1) Before existence, an individual is pure non-existence and has no distinction, so it cannot be known. (2) If God knew things before they occur, then their occurrence would be necessary (to avoid changing knowledge), which would imply determinism (جبر) and negate human free will. The author's answer to the first is that we ourselves know non-existent individuals before they occur (e.g., knowing the sunrise tomorrow), so it is not impossible. The answer to the second is to accept the consequence: what God knows to occur is indeed necessary of occurrence. The editorial note (أقول) adds: this necessity is a 'subsequent necessity' (وجوب لاحق) not a 'prior necessity' (وجوب سابق) that compels. God knows things as they will be, and His will corresponds to His knowledge, but this does not create compulsion because God knows those acts as occurring from the agent's choice. The note also references an earlier discussion about the possibility of knowing non-existents. Thus, the argument that foreknowledge implies determinism is deflected by distinguishing types of necessity.

Key Terms
جزئيات
ماهية
معدوم
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existent things cannot be known because they have no reality.
Correction: The author shows that we can know non-existent individuals before they exist, like knowing tomorrow's sunrise, so non-existence does not prevent knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that non-existent particulars cannot be known?
A) By denying that God knows them.
B) By citing our own knowledge of future non-existents.
C) By arguing that non-existence has no distinction.
D) By saying God only knows essences.

Key Takeaway
The objection that non-existent particulars are unknowable is refuted by our own knowledge of future events like sunrise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God knows every detail of your future—including your sins, your moments of faith, and your death—yet you still lie awake worrying about tomorrow, what does that reveal about the sincerity of your belief in His all-encompassing knowledge? Will you today entrust one specific unknown outcome to Him and refuse to fear it?
?
The objection in this lesson assumes that non-existents cannot be known, but the refutation shows that even you know tomorrow's sunrise. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God is ignorant of your future? What would change if you lived as though He has known every moment since eternity?
?
You claim to believe that God knows everything, but do you actually want Him to know every ugly thought and hidden sin? If not, you are denying His attribute of 'ilm. What are you afraid He sees, and how can you surrender that fear in repentance today?

Does Foreknowledge Lead to Determinism?
Razi presents the second proof and its answer with the editor's clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that foreknowledge implies necessity and determinism, and the resolution that the necessity is subsequent, not prior, thus avoiding compulsion.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge does not force the act; it simply knows what will happen freely.

The second proof takes a different approach: if God knows the future, then that future must happen, which seems to eliminate free will. Razi presents this challenge and the theological response that distinguishes between types of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the logical chain: foreknowledge → non-occurrence leads to ignorance → impossible → occurrence necessary → compulsion.
The consequence of compulsion (جبر) is highlighted.
2
Understand the determinism argument
The objector argues that if God knows future events, they become necessary, and then no free will is possible—animals become like inanimate objects.
This is a classical objection against divine foreknowledge.
3
Grasp the answer: subsequent necessity
The author accepts that what God knows to occur is necessary, but the editorial note clarifies that this necessity is 'subsequent' (وجوب لاحق), not 'prior' (وجوب سابق). God knows the act as it will occur from the agent's choice, so there is no compulsion.
This distinction is key to reconciling foreknowledge with free will.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine foreknowledge compels human actions.
Correction: The author argues that foreknowledge only means God knows what will happen, not that He forces it. The necessity is subsequent to the act, not prior.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in the answer to the second proof?
A) Between prior and subsequent necessity.
B) Between knowledge and will.
C) Between necessary and possible.
D) Between eternal and temporal.

Key Takeaway
The necessity that follows from divine foreknowledge is a subsequent necessity (وجوب لاحق), meaning God knows the act as it will be done by free choice, not that He compels it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt trapped by the thought that God already knows what you will do, as if your choices are illusions? How does distinguishing between prior and subsequent necessity change the way you see your next decision? Will you act today with the full awareness that your choice is real and known?
?
If God knows that you will sin tomorrow, do you still hold yourself accountable for that sin? Or does that knowledge make you feel excused? How does this lesson force you to take responsibility for your actions despite God's foreknowledge?
?
Consider a time you made a difficult moral choice. Did you secretly wish that God had predestined it so you could avoid responsibility? After learning that the necessity is only logical, what will you do differently when faced with a similar test?

The Three Proofs Against Knowing the Infinite
Razi enumerates three proofs for the impossibility of knowing the infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs used to argue that God cannot know an infinite number of things.

Core Idea
Infinite knowns lead to logical contradictions.

This passage presents the strongest objection against divine omniscience over infinite particulars. The author lists three logical proofs, each building on concepts of finitude and distinction. Let's examine them carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage containing the three proofs straight through. Notice how each proof is constructed with a conditional chain.
The proofs are numbered 'first', 'second', 'third'.
2
Identify the logical structure of each
For each proof, identify the premises and the conclusion. The first uses increase/decrease, the second uses distinctness, the third uses distinct acts of knowledge.
Each proof aims to show that what is known must be finite.
3
Consider why these proofs seem compelling
Think about the intuitive appeal: if something increases, it seems finite; if it is distinct, it seems bounded; if knowledge of each item is separate, then infinite items would require infinite knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من أنكر كونه عالما بما لا نهاية له واحتج بثلاثة أوجه الأول أن المعلومات تتطرق إليها الزيادة والنقصان فإن بعضها أقل من كلها وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه فالمعلوم متناه . الثانى أن كل ما كان معلوما فهو متميز عن غيره وكل ما كان متميزا عن غيره فغيره خارج عنه وكل ما كان غيره خارجا عنه فهو متناه فكل معلوم متناه فما ليس بمتناه وجب أن لا يكون معلوما. الثالث أن العلم بكل المعلوم مغاير للعلم بغيره بدليل أنه يصح أن يعلم كون الشيء عالما بشيء آخر الجول بكونه عالا بغيره والمعلوم غير المجهول فلو كانت المعلومات غير متناهية لكانت العلوم غير متناهية فهناك موجودات غير متناهية وهو محال والجواب عن الأول أن تطرق الزيادة والنقصان إلى شيء لا يدل على التناهى وعن الثاني أن المتميز كل واحد منهما وهو متناه وعن الثالث أن العلم واحد لكن نسبته غير متناهية وهذا ضعيف لأن الشعور بالشيء إذا كان لا يتحقق إلا مع هذه النسب فهذه النسب إن لم تسكن موجودة لم يكن العلم موجوداً وإن كانت موجودة عاد الإلزام وقد ذكرنا أن الأستاذ أبا سهل الصعلوكي التزمه (٢) أقول : حجتهم الأولى تدل على امتناع مالا نهاية له مطلقا وليس لها تعلق بالمعلومات التي لا نهاية لها من حيث كونها معلومة . وجوابه عن قوله المعلوم متميز عن غيره والمتميز متناه بأن المتميز إن كان واحداً منها وهو متناه غير، لأن الدعوى أن الله تعالى عالم بغير المتناهى فغير المتناهى عنده معلوم فهو متميز، ويسلم أن كل متميز صحيح متناه يلزمه أن غير المتناهى متناه، والصواب أن يمنع الكبرى فإن المتناهي وغير المتناهى معلومان ولا يلزم منه تناهى غير المتناهى . حيث هي المعقولات لا من حيث هي جزئيات متغيرة، قالوا المدرك للجزئيات الزمانية من حيث هي متغيرة يجب أن يكون زمانيا ذا آلة قابلا للتغير وهو شبيه بالإحساس وما يجرى مجراه، وهو تعالى منزه عن هذا النوع من الإدراك، كما أنه منزه عن الإحساس والذوق والشم والإشارة الحسية، هذا هو مذهبهم
And among them are those who denied that He is knowing of what has no end, and argued with three proofs: First, that information is subject to increase and decrease, for some of it is less than all of it, and everything that is so is finite, so the known is finite. Second, that everything that is known is distinguished from other than it, and everything distinguished from other than it has other than it outside it, and everything that has other than it outside it is finite. So every known is finite, so what is not finite must not be known. Third, that knowledge of each known is different from knowledge of another, as evidenced by the fact that it is valid to know that a thing is knowing of something else and ignorant of another, and the known is different from the unknown. So if information were infinite, then knowledges would be infinite, so there would be infinite existing things, which is impossible. The answer to the first: That subjection to increase and decrease does not indicate finiteness. To the second: That the distinguished, each one of them, is finite. To the third: That knowledge is one but its relations are infinite; but this is weak because awareness of a thing, if it is only realized with these relations, then if these relations are not existent, knowledge would not exist, and if they are existent, the objection returns. And we have mentioned that the master Abu Sahl al-Su'luki accepted it. (2) I say: Their first proof indicates the impossibility of the infinite absolutely, and it has no connection to information that are infinite insofar as they are known. And his answer to his statement 'the known is distinguished from other than it and the distinguished is finite' is that the distinguished, if it is one of them, is finite, because the claim is that God, exalted, is knowing of the infinite, so the infinite is to Him known, so it is distinguished, and one concedes that every distinguished is finite; this would entail that the infinite is finite. The correct view is to deny the major premise, for the finite and the infinite are both known and this does not entail the finitude of the infinite. Where they are intelligibles, not insofar as they are changing particulars, they said: The perceiver of temporal particulars insofar as they are changing must be temporal, having a tool susceptible to change, and it is akin to sensation and what runs its course. And He, exalted, is transcendent above this type of perception, just as He is transcendent above sensation, taste, smell, and sensory indication. This is their doctrine.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection: some deny that God can know an infinite number of things, using three proofs. The first proof: known things are subject to increase and decrease, so they must be finite. The second: every known thing is distinct from others, and that which is distinct has something else outside it, so it is bounded and finite. The third: knowledge of each known is different from knowledge of another, so if there were infinite knowns, there would be infinite knowledges, which is impossible. The author's answers: to the first, being subject to increase/decrease does not prove finiteness (e.g., numbers can increase without bound). To the second, each individual known is finite, but the set can be infinite. To the third, the author mentions a view that knowledge is one but its relations are infinite, but dismisses it as weak because the relations would need to exist. He notes that Abu Sahl al-Su'luki accepted this objection. The editorial note (أقول) critiques the first proof as irrelevant to knowns as such. It then explains that the second proof can be refuted by denying that every distinct thing is finite (the major premise): the finite and infinite are both known, and knowing the infinite does not make it finite. The note then adds a final paragraph (perhaps a continuation of the note or a summary of the philosophers' view) stating that God knows things as intellectibles (معقولات), not as changing particulars; perceiving changing particulars requires a temporal, changeable faculty, which God transcends. This is the doctrine of the philosophers.

Key Terms
لا نهاية
متناه
متميز
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which proof argues that if knowledge of each known is different, then infinite knowns would require infinite knowledges?
A) First proof
B) Second proof
C) Third proof
D) All three

Key Takeaway
These three proofs challenge divine omniscience by arguing that infinite knowns would violate logical principles of finitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah as all-knowing, do you ever consider that the infinite might be logically problematic? Does this proof make you feel insecure about His omniscience? How would you defend the belief that Allah knows everything to someone who uses this argument?
?
Razi argues that each known requires a distinct act of knowledge. Have you ever thought of Allah's knowledge as being fragmented into many acts? Does this diminish your awe of His single, perfect knowledge? What does this say about the limits of human logic when applied to the Divine?

The Author's Answers to the Three Proofs
Razi provides answers to the three proofs and critiques the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's responses to each proof and why the third answer is considered weak.

Core Idea
Increase and decrease do not prove finitude, nor does distinction; but the third answer is problematic.

After presenting the objection, the author provides concise responses. Each answer targets the core assumption of the proof. However, the third answer is noted as weak and is dismissed.

Guided Path
1
Read the answers
Read the Arabic passage containing the author's answers to the three proofs. Notice how each answer is brief.
2
Match each answer to its proof
Identify which answer corresponds to which proof. The first answer addresses increase/decrease, the second addresses distinction, the third addresses distinct knowledges.
The third answer includes a critique that it is weak.
3
Understand why the third answer is weak
The author explains that the third answer (knowledge is one but relations are infinite) fails because the relations would have to exist, leading back to the original objection.
The author also mentions that Abu Sahl al-Su'luki accepted the objection.

The Editorial Critique and Divine Knowledge of Intelligibles
Editorial critique of the proofs and statement of the correct doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editorial critique of the proofs and the correct doctrine that God knows things as intelligibles, not as changing particulars.

Core Idea
The infinite can be known as a unified intelligible, not as discrete changing particulars.

The editorial note offers a deeper critique of the first two proofs and clarifies the philosophers' position: God's knowledge is of intelligibles, not temporal particulars. This distinction resolves the difficulty of knowing the infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the editorial note
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'أقول' to the end. Notice that it critiques the proofs and then states the correct doctrine.
The note is a separate commentary from the original author.
2
Understand the critique of the major premise
The note denies the major premise that every distinct thing is finite. It says that the infinite is known and distinct yet not finite.
This directly refutes the second proof's logic.
3
Grasp the distinction between intelligibles and particulars
The note concludes that God knows things as intelligibles (universals), not as changing particulars. Perceiving changing particulars requires a temporal faculty, which God does not have.
This is the philosophers' doctrine.

He notes that Abu Sahl al-Su'luki accepted this objection. The editorial note (أقول) critiques the first proof as irrelevant to knowns as such. It then explains that the second proof can be refuted by denying that every distinct thing is finite (the major premise): the finite and infinite are both known, and knowing the infinite does not make it finite. The note then adds a final paragraph (perhaps a continuation of the note or a summary of the philosophers' view) stating that God knows things as intellectibles (معقولات), not as changing particulars; perceiving changing particulars requires a temporal, changeable faculty, which God transcends. This is the doctrine of the philosophers.

جزئيات

متناه

Misconception: Some think that knowing the infinite requires enumerating all particulars, which would be impossible.
Correction: God knows the infinite as a unified intelligible, not as a set of discrete changing particulars.

Checkpoint
What is the correct response to the second proof's claim that everything distinct is finite?
A) Accept the proof and conclude God cannot know the infinite
B) Deny that the infinite is distinct
C) Deny the major premise that every distinct thing is finite
D) Say that the infinite is not really known

Key Takeaway
God knows the infinite as a single intelligible, not as many changing particulars, thus resolving the logical difficulty.

God's Knowledge of Particulars: Objection and Response
Al-Razi presents two proofs against divine knowledge of particulars and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God cannot know particulars before they occur, and how it is refuted by rational arguments.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not depend on the existence of its objects.

Al-Razi presents a theological challenge: if God only knows universals, how can He know each particular thing before it exists? This passage examines two arguments against divine foreknowledge of particulars and shows why they fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is divided into two objections and two answers.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage presents two objections. The first concerns the non-existence of particulars before occurrence. The second concerns the necessity of occurrence if God knows them.
The first objection is epistemological, the second is about determinism.
3
Understand the refutations
The first refutation uses an analogy: humans know future particulars (e.g., sunrise). The second accepts the logical consequence but denies coercion.
The second response is subtle: acceptance of necessity does not imply compulsion.
4
Reflect on the logical necessity point
Consider how the necessity of occurrence based on God's knowledge is logical, not coercive. This distinction is key to rejecting determinism.
This connects to broader debates on divine foreknowledge and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من زعم أنه لا يعلم الجرئيات إلا عند وقوعها وقبل ذلك فإنه لا يعلم إلا الماهية واحتج بوجهين الأول أن المعلوم متميز والشخص قبل وجوده نفى محض فلا يكون فى نفسه متميزا فلا يصح أن يكون معلوما الثاني أنه تعالى لو علم الأشياء قبل وقوعها فكل ما علم فهو واجب الوقوع لأن عدم وقوعه يشفى إلى انقلاب العلم جهلا وهو محال والمؤدى إلى المحال محال فعدم وقوعه محال فوقوعه واجب وحينئذ يلزم الحر [؟] وإن لا يتمكن الحيوان من فعل أصلا بل يكون كالجماد لأن ما علم وقوعه فهو واجب وما علم عدمه فهو ممتنع والجواب عن الأول أنه منقوض يعلمنا بالمعدومات الشخصية قبل وقوعها كعلمنا بطلوع الشمس غدا وعن الثاني بالتزام أن ما علم الله تعالى وقوعه فهو واجب الوقوع
And among them is the one who claimed that He does not know particulars except at their occurrence, and before that He knows only the essence. He argued with two aspects: First, that the known is distinguished, and the person before its existence is pure non-existence, so it is not distinguished in itself, so it cannot be known. Second, that if He, exalted, knew things before their occurrence, then everything He knows is necessary of occurrence, because its non-occurrence would lead to knowledge turning into ignorance, which is impossible, and what leads to the impossible is impossible, so its non-occurrence is impossible, so its occurrence is necessary. And then coercion [al-hurr?] would follow, and the animal would not be able to act at all, but would be like an inanimate object, because what is known to occur is necessary, and what is known not to occur is impossible. The answer to the first is that it is refuted by our knowledge of personal non-existents before their occurrence, like our knowledge of the sunrise tomorrow. And to the second, by accepting that what God, exalted, knows to occur is necessary of occurrence.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector denies that God knows particulars before they exist, offering two proofs: First, a non-existent particular has no distinction in itself, so it cannot be known. Second, if God knew things before occurrence, that knowledge would necessitate their occurrence (otherwise knowledge would become ignorance), leading to determinism (al-jabr), making creatures unable to act. The refutation answers the first by noting that humans themselves know future particulars (e.g., tomorrow's sunrise), so the objection is invalid. The answer to the second accepts the consequence—that what God knows to occur is necessary—but this does not imply coercion because it is a logical necessity based on God's knowledge.

Key Terms
الجرئيات
المعلوم
الوجوب
الحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge of future events implies that humans are forced to act, negating free will.
Correction: Necessity based on God's knowledge is logical (if known, it must occur), not causal coercion. Humans still act voluntarily within that framework.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection in this passage, why cannot God know a particular before it occurs?
A) Because particulars are infinite and cannot be encompassed.
B) Because a non-existent particular has no distinction in itself.
C) Because God only knows universals and essences.
D) Because knowledge of the future is logically impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God cannot know non-existents fails because even humans know future particulars, and the necessity implied by God's knowledge is logical, not coercive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that since God already knows what you will do, your choices don't really matter? How does the lesson's distinction between logical necessity and coercion challenge that excuse?

?
If you truly believed that the necessity of God's knowledge is not a chain but a mirror of your own real choices, would you still blame your circumstances or your upbringing for your faults? What step can you take right now to own your actions without that crutch?
