Three Arguments Against Infinite Divine Knowledge
Al-Razi gives three objections against infinite divine knowledge and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical arguments that attempt to deny God's knowledge of infinite things and the refutations provided by Al-Razi.

Core Idea
Three arguments challenge infinite divine knowledge, but each has a counter-argument.

Al-Razi presents three logical arguments that attempt to prove that God cannot know infinite things. He then provides refutations for each, showing the complexity of theological reasoning. This passage delves into the nature of knowledge, infinity, and distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the structure of the three proofs and refutations become clear.
The passage is divided into three proofs followed by their respective refutations.
2
Identify the first proof and its refutation
The first proof argues that known things admit increase and decrease, so they are finite. The refutation says increase and decrease do not necessarily imply finitude.
Notice that the refutation challenges the premise that change implies finiteness.
3
Identify the second proof and its refutation
The second proof argues that everything known is distinct from others, and what is distinct has something outside it, so it is finite. The refutation states that the thing being distinct is itself finite, but this does not apply to the infinite.
The refutation points out a logical gap: distinctness does not entail finitude for the infinite.
4
Identify the third proof and its refutation
The third proof argues that knowledge of each thing is distinct, so infinite things would require infinite knowledges, which is impossible. The refutation suggests knowledge is one but its relations are infinite, though Al-Razi notes this answer is weak.
Pay attention to the weak refutation and the acceptance by Abu Sahl al-Su'luki.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how each proof is refuted and why the third refutation is considered weak. What does this tell us about theological argumentation?
This shows that even respected scholars debate and accept consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من أنكر كونه عالما بما لا نهاية له واحتج بثلاثة أوجه الأول أن المعلومات تتطرق إليها الزيادة والنقصان فإن بعضها أقل من كلها وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه فالمعلوم متناه . الثانى أن كل ما كان معلوما فهو متميز عن غيره وكل ما كان متميزا عن غيره فغيره خارج عنه وكل ما كان غيره خارجا عنه فهو متناه فكل معلوم متناه فما ليس بمتناه وجب أن لا يكون معلوما. الثالث أن العلم بكل المعلوم مغاير للعلم بغيره بدليل أنه يصح أن يعلم كون الشيء عالما بشيء آخر الجول بكونه عالا بغيره والمعلوم غير المجهول فلو كانت المعلومات غير متناهية لكانت العلوم غير متناهية فهناك موجودات غير متناهية وهو محال والجواب عن الأول أن تطرق الزيادة والنقصان إلى شيء لا يدل على النتاهى وعن الثاني أن المتميز كل واحد منهما وهو متناه وعن الثالث أن العلم واحد لكن نسبته غير متناهية وهذا ضعيف لأن الشعور بالشيء إذا كان لا يتحقق إلا مع هذه النسب فهذه النسب إن لم تسكن موجودة لم يكن العلم موجوداً وإن كانت موجودة عاد الإلزام وقد ذكرنا أن الأستاذ أبا سهل الصعلوكي التزمه
And among them is the one who denied that He is knowledgeable of what has no end and argued with three aspects: First, that information admits increase and decrease, for some of them are less than all of them, and everything that is like that is finite, so the known is finite. Second, that everything that is known is distinguished from other than it, and everything that is distinguished from other than it, other than it is outside it, and everything that has other than it outside it is finite, so every known is finite, so what is not finite must not be known. Third, that the knowledge of each known is different from the knowledge of other than it, as evidenced by it being valid to know that something is knowledgeable of another thing [unclear] and it being knowledgeable of other than it, and the known is not the unknown. So if the objects of knowledge were infinite, the knowledges would be infinite, so there would be infinite existents, which is impossible. The answer to the first is that the admission of increase and decrease to a thing does not indicate finitude. And to the second, that each of them is distinguished, and it is finite. And to the third, that knowledge is one, but its relation is infinite. And this is weak, because the awareness of a thing, if it is not realized except with these relations, then if these relations are not existing, knowledge would not exist, and if they are existing, the implication returns. And we have mentioned that the master Abu Sahl al-Su'luki accepted it.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs against God knowing infinite things, with refutations. First proof: Known things admit increase and decrease, so they are finite; thus all known things are finite. Refutation: Increase and decrease do not entail finitude. Second proof: Everything known is distinct from others, and what is distinct has something outside it, so it is finite. Refutation: The thing that is distinct is itself finite, but the argument does not apply to the infinite. Third proof: Knowledge of each thing is different from knowledge of another; if things were infinite, knowledges would be infinite, leading to an infinite number of existents, which is impossible. Refutation: Knowledge is one, but its relations are infinite. However, this answer is weak because if the relations are necessary for knowledge, they must exist, leading back to the problem. The author notes that Abu Sahl al-Su'luki accepted this consequence.

Correction: Al-Razi shows that the arguments are not decisive and are subject to refutation; some scholars accepted the objection, but it remains a matter of debate.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The arguments against infinite divine knowledge are not definitive; they are countered by refutations, though some are stronger than others.

The Infinite Regress Objection to Divine Omniscience
Al-Razi presents the infinite regress argument against God knowing all things and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the infinite regress objection to God's omniscience and how the response distinguishes between substantial attributes and non-substantial relations.

Core Idea
Infinite regress only applies to non-substantial relations, not to God's single attribute of knowledge.

This passage from al-Razi's Muhassal presents a rational objection to the claim that God knows all things: if He knows each thing, He must also know His knowledge of that thing, leading to an infinite chain of knowledges. Al-Razi then refutes the objection by distinguishing between the attribute of knowledge itself and its relations, showing that the infinity is only in non-substantial attachments. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage that presents the infinite regress argument. Notice how every act of knowledge would require another level of knowledge.
The objector claims this leads to infinite attributes.
2
Consider a possible answer
Then a possible response is suggested: that knowledge of a thing is the same as knowledge of knowledge. The passage immediately refutes this.
This is a common but flawed reply.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Razi explains why they are different: knowledge of a thing is a relation to the thing, while knowledge of knowledge is a relation between knowledge and its object.
They are distinct relations.
4
Examine the final answer
The passage concludes that infinity is accepted in relations, which are non-substantial; only the knowledge itself is a single attribute.
This solves the problem without making attributes infinite.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this distinguishes between the attribute (thubuti) and its relations (ghayr thubutiyya).
This is a key concept in kalam epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من أنكر كونه عالماً بجميع المعلومات واحتج بأنه لو علم جميع المعلومات لمكان إذا علم شيئا علم كونه عالما به وعلم أيضاً كونه عالما بكونه عالما ويترتب هناك مراتب غير متناهية ، وإذا كانت معلوماته غير متناهية وله بحسب كل معلوم مراتب غير متناهية كانت الصفات غير متناهية الأمرة واحدة بل مراراً غير متناهية . فإن قلت العلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالعلم به . قلت : هذا باطل لأن العلم بالشيء إضافة إلى الشيء والعلم بالعلم بالشيء إضافة بين العلم وبين العلم بالشيء والإضافة إلى الشيء غير الإضافة إلى غيره . والجواب : أن لانهاية في النسب والتعلقات وهى أمور غير ثبوتية إنما الثابت هو العلم وهو صفة واحدة وفيه الإشكال بالذي تقدم .
And among them is the one who denied that He is knowledgeable of all information and argued that if He knew all information, then when He knows something, He knows that He is knowledgeable of it, and also knows that He is knowledgeable of His being knowledgeable, and there arise infinite levels. And if His objects of knowledge are infinite and He has, according to each object of knowledge, infinite levels, then the attributes would be infinite—the matter being one—but rather infinite times. If you say: knowledge of the thing is the same as knowledge of knowledge of it. I say: this is false, because knowledge of the thing is an addition to the thing, and knowledge of knowledge of the thing is an addition between knowledge and knowledge of the thing, and the addition to the thing is not the addition to other than it. And the answer: that there is infinity in the relations and attachments, and they are non-substantial matters; only the knowledge is established, and it is a single attribute, and in it is the problem mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents an objection that if God knows all things, then He also knows that He knows, leading to infinite regress of knowledges. The objector argues that if God knows something, He also knows His knowledge of it, and so on infinitely, resulting in infinite attributes, which is impossible. A possible response is that knowledge of a thing is identical to knowledge of that knowledge. The author refutes this: knowledge of a thing is a relation to the thing, while knowledge of knowledge is a relation between knowledge and knowledge of the thing, so they are different. The final answer is that there is infinity in relations and attachments, which are non-substantial (ghayr thubutiyya); only the knowledge itself is a single attribute. But the problem remains the same as before.

Correction: The answer shows that the infinity is only in non-substantial relations, not in the attribute of knowledge itself.

A) It leads to multiple divine attributes.
B) It implies that God learns gradually.
C) It requires infinite past events.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection is resolved by recognizing that only non-substantial relations are infinite, not God's single attribute of knowledge.

The Basis of Divine Omnipotence: Possibility as the Criterion
Al-Razi establishes the criterion for being an object of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the basis for something being a possible object of God's power is its intrinsic possibility, which is shared by all possible things, and why this entails God's power over all possible things.

Core Idea
Possibility is the common basis for all objects of God's power.

Al-Razi begins by stating the doctrine that God is powerful over all possible things, contrary to various sects. He then identifies the criterion for something being a possible object of divine power: it must be possible in itself, not necessary or impossible. Since possibility is a common attribute of all possible things, they are all equally suitable for God's power. If His power were limited, a specific would be needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice that al-Razi starts by stating his school's position and then gives the rationale.
2
Identify the three modal categories
The passage mentions three: possibility (imkan), necessity (wujub), and impossibility (imtina'). Understand that only possibility allows for being an object of power.
3
Grasp the concept of shared attribute
Possibility is a description shared among all possible things, so they are all equally valid as objects of God's power.
4
Understand the implication
Since all possible things share this attribute, God's power cannot be restricted without a cause. This establishes that He is powerful over all possible things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكنات ( مسألة : مذهب أصحابنا أن الله تعالى قادر على كل المقدورات خلافا لجميع الفرق ) انا أن ما لاجله صح فى البعض أن يكون مقدور الله تعالى هو الإمكان ، لأن ما عداه إما الوجوب وإما الامتناع وهما يحيلان المقدورية لكن الإمكان وصف مشترك فيه بين الممكنات فيكون الكل مشتركا في صحة مقدورية الله تعالى ، فلو اختصت قادريته بالبعض افتقر إلى المخصص، وإذا ثبت أنه قادر على جميع وجب أن لا يوجد شيء من الممكنات إلا بقدرته إذ لو فرضنا شيئا آخر مؤثرا لكانا إذا اجتمعا على ذلك الممكن فإما أن يقمع ذلك الممكن بهما فيجتمع على الأثر الواحد مؤثران مستقلان وهو محال أو لا يقع بواحد منهما وهو محال لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذا وقوعه بذاك فمالم يوجد وقوعه بهذا لا يمتنع وقوعه بذاك، فلو امتنع وقوعه بهذا أو ذاك لزم وقوعه بهذا وذاك حتى يكون وقوعه بكل واحد منهما مانعا بالآخر وذلك محال ، وإما أن يقع بأحدهما دون الآخر وهو محال لأن كل واحد لما كان مستقلا بالتأثير كان وقوعه بأحدهما دون الآخر ترجيحا لأحد طرق الممكن على الآخر بلا مرجح وهو محال ، فثبت أن الممكنات واقع بقدرة الله تعالى وتعلقاته
The possible things (Issue: The doctrine of our companions that God, exalted, is powerful over all the empowered, contrary to all sects.) [We say] that that for which it is valid in some to be a power object of God, exalted, is possibility, because what is other than it is either necessity or impossibility, and they both render power impossible. But possibility is a description shared among possible things, so all share in the validity of being a power object of God, exalted. So if His power were restricted to some, it would need a specific. And when it is established that He is powerful over all, it is necessary that nothing of the possible things exists except by His power. For if we suppose another effective agent, then when they both come together on that possible, then either that possible occurs by both, so two independent agents meet on one effect, which is impossible; or it does not occur by either, which is impossible because the preventer of its occurrence by this is its occurrence by that; so as long as its occurrence by this does not exist, its occurrence by that is not prevented. So if its occurrence by this or that were prevented, its occurrence by this and that would follow, so that its occurrence by each would be a preventer of the other, and that is impossible. Or it occurs by one without the other, which is impossible because each, since it is independent in effect, its occurrence by one without the other would be a preference of one of the ways of the possible over the other without a preferrer, which is impossible. So it is established that the possible things occur by the power of God, exalted, and His attachments.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is powerful over all possible things (al-mumkinat). The author states the position of his school (Ash'ari) that God is powerful over all that can be empowered, contrary to various groups. The reason some things are possible objects of God's power is their possibility (imkan), as opposed to necessity or impossibility, which preclude being a power object. Possibility is a common attribute among all possible things, so all are equally suitable for God's power. If His power were limited to some, a specific would be needed. Once established that He is powerful over all, it follows that nothing possible occurs except by His power. If another effective agent were supposed, when both act on the same possible, either (a) the effect occurs by both—two independent agents on one effect, impossible; (b) it occurs by neither—impossible because prevention by one would require the other; (c) it occurs by one without the other—impossible because that would be arbitrary preference without a determining factor. Thus, all possible things occur by God's power and His attachments.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الإمكان

الامتناع

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's power extends to all possible things, then no excuse remains for not trusting Him in every situation. What one area of your life do you still act as if God cannot change, and what will you do about it before the end of today?

The Impossibility of Secondary Independent Agents
Al-Razi disproves the existence of independent secondary agents through parity of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that nothing possible exists except by God's power, by showing that the assumption of another independent agent leads to impossible consequences.

Core Idea
No possible thing can occur without God's power, as dual-agency leads to absurdity.

Having established that God is powerful over all possible things, al-Razi now argues that they must all occur only by His power. He supposes another effective agent and shows that three scenarios—both acting, neither acting, one acting—are all impossible. Therefore, all possible things are dependent on God's power alone.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The passage asserts that nothing possible exists except by God's power. This is derived from the earlier conclusion that God is powerful over all possible things.
2
Consider the hypothetical
Suppose there is another effective agent besides God. Then when both act on a possible thing, analyze the possible outcomes.
3
Evaluate each outcome
Three possibilities: the effect occurs by both, by neither, or by one. Each is shown to be impossible: two independent agents on one effect is impossible; occurrence by neither is impossible because prevention requires the other; occurrence by one is arbitrary preference without a cause.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since all alternatives are impossible, the only valid conclusion is that all possible things occur by God's power alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكنات ( مسألة : مذهب أصحابنا أن الله تعالى قادر على كل المقدورات خلافا لجميع الفرق ) انا أن ما لاجله صح فى البعض أن يكون مقدور الله تعالى هو الإمكان ، لأن ما عداه إما الوجوب وإما الامتناع وهما يحيلان المقدورية لكن الإمكان وصف مشترك فيه بين الممكنات فيكون الكل مشتركا في صحة مقدورية الله تعالى ، فلو اختصت قادريته بالبعض افتقر إلى المخصص، وإذا ثبت أنه قادر على جميع وجب أن لا يوجد شيء من الممكنات إلا بقدرته إذ لو فرضنا شيئا آخر مؤثرا لكانا إذا اجتمعا على ذلك الممكن فإما أن يقمع ذلك الممكن بهما فيجتمع على الأثر الواحد مؤثران مستقلان وهو محال أو لا يقع بواحد منهما وهو محال لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذا وقوعه بذاك فمالم يوجد وقوعه بهذا لا يمتنع وقوعه بذاك، فلو امتنع وقوعه بهذا أو ذاك لزم وقوعه بهذا وذاك حتى يكون وقوعه بكل واحد منهما مانعا بالآخر وذلك محال ، وإما أن يقع بأحدهما دون الآخر وهو محال لأن كل واحد لما كان مستقلا بالتأثير كان وقوعه بأحدهما دون الآخر ترجيحا لأحد طرق الممكن على الآخر بلا مرجح وهو محال ، فثبت أن الممكنات واقع بقدرة الله تعالى وتعلقاته
The possible things (Issue: The doctrine of our companions that God, exalted, is powerful over all the empowered, contrary to all sects.) [We say] that that for which it is valid in some to be a power object of God, exalted, is possibility, because what is other than it is either necessity or impossibility, and they both render power impossible. But possibility is a description shared among possible things, so all share in the validity of being a power object of God, exalted. So if His power were restricted to some, it would need a specific. And when it is established that He is powerful over all, it is necessary that nothing of the possible things exists except by His power. For if we suppose another effective agent, then when they both come together on that possible, then either that possible occurs by both, so two independent agents meet on one effect, which is impossible; or it does not occur by either, which is impossible because the preventer of its occurrence by this is its occurrence by that; so as long as its occurrence by this does not exist, its occurrence by that is not prevented. So if its occurrence by this or that were prevented, its occurrence by this and that would follow, so that its occurrence by each would be a preventer of the other, and that is impossible. Or it occurs by one without the other, which is impossible because each, since it is independent in effect, its occurrence by one without the other would be a preference of one of the ways of the possible over the other without a preferrer, which is impossible. So it is established that the possible things occur by the power of God, exalted, and His attachments.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is powerful over all possible things (al-mumkinat). The author states the position of his school (Ash'ari) that God is powerful over all that can be empowered, contrary to various groups. The reason some things are possible objects of God's power is their possibility (imkan), as opposed to necessity or impossibility, which preclude being a power object. Possibility is a common attribute among all possible things, so all are equally suitable for God's power. If His power were limited to some, a specific would be needed. Once established that He is powerful over all, it follows that nothing possible occurs except by His power. If another effective agent were supposed, when both act on the same possible, either (a) the effect occurs by both—two independent agents on one effect, impossible; (b) it occurs by neither—impossible because prevention by one would require the other; (c) it occurs by one without the other—impossible because that would be arbitrary preference without a determining factor. Thus, all possible things occur by God's power and His attachments.

Key Terms
قادر
مقدور
تأثير
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that secondary causes (like fire, humans) can produce effects independently of God.
Correction: This proof shows that any claim of an independent secondary agent leads to logical absurdities; all possible effects are directly from God's power.

Checkpoint
In the proof against another agent, which scenario is considered impossible because it would involve two independent agents on one effect?
A) The effect occurs by both agents
B) The effect occurs by neither agent
C) The effect occurs by one agent alone
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
All possible events and objects are directly created by God's power; no other agent can act independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every single event in your life—from the air you breathe to the thoughts you think—is directly created by God's power alone, how does that change your sense of dependence on Him each moment? What would it mean to truly live as if nothing happens without His immediate will?
?
When you attribute a success or a failure to your own effort, are you implicitly denying God's sole agency in that outcome? How can you train yourself to see God's direct hand in every detail, even those you feel you accomplished?
?
Razi's proof leaves no room for any secondary independent cause. Is there any area of your life where you still act as if something or someone has power independent of God? What would it take to uproot that hidden shirk from your heart today?

The Objection of Infinite Knowledge and Its Answer
Imam Razi presents an objection about infinite divine knowledge and answers by distinguishing relations from essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that divine knowledge leads to infinite attributes by distinguishing between the attribute itself and its non-existential relations.

Core Idea
Infinite relations do not multiply the attribute; they are non-existential.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection against the doctrine of God's all-encompassing knowledge. The objector argues that if God knows everything, then for each act of knowing there would be an infinite regress of meta-knowledge, leading to an infinite number of attributes. Imam Razi responds by distinguishing between the attribute of knowledge itself—which is one—and the relations that arise from it, which are non-existential and therefore do not create multiplicity. This lesson unpacks the objection, the attempted counter, and the final answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that infinite regress follows from God knowing all things: for each known thing, God must know that He knows it, and so on ad infinitum.
Notice the objector concludes this would make the attributes infinite in number.
2
Examine the attempted counter
Note the suggestion that knowing a thing is identical to knowing that you know it. Razi refutes this by showing they are different relations.
3
Grasp the key answer
Razi distinguishes between the attribute of knowledge (thubooti) and the relations (nisaab) which are non-existential (ghayr thubootiyya). The infinite regress occurs only in relations, not in the attribute itself.
4
Consider the final remark
Razi notes that even this answer faces a similar objection, and he seems to waver, ultimately favoring Abu Sahl's view.
This shows the complexity of the issue within kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عالماً بجميع المعلومات واحتج بأنه لو علم جميع المعلومات لمكان إذا علم شيئا علم كونه عالما به وعلم أيضاً كونه عالما بكونه عالما ويترتب هناك مراتب غير متناهية ، وإذا كانت معلوماته غير متناهية وله بحسب كل معلوم مراتب غير متناهية كانت الصفات غير متناهية الأمرة واحدة بل مراراً غير متناهية .
فإن قلت العلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالعلم به .
قلت : هذا باطل لأن العلم بالشيء إضافة إلى الشيء والعلم بالعلم بالشيء إضافة بين العلم وبين العلم بالشيء والإضافة إلى الشيء غير الإضافة إلى غيره .
والجواب : أن لانهاية في النسب والتعلقات وهى أمور غير ثبوتية إنما الثابت هو العلم وهو صفة واحدة وفيه الإشكال بالذي تقدم .
وما أجاب به عن الثالث يدل على حيرته وإن ذكر فيها مر أن الحق أن العلم أمر إضافي وههنا جمله ما واحدا متكثر النسب ، وصرح من قبل بكون النسب غير وجودية ثم لما تحير فيه صوب قول أبي سهل تعريضا.
Knowing all things, and he argued that if He knows all things, then when He knows something, He knows that He knows it, and He also knows that He knows that He knows it, and there follows an infinite number of levels. And if His objects of knowledge are infinite, and for each object of knowledge there are infinite levels, then the attributes would be infinite, either [as] one or multiple times infinite. If you say: knowledge of a thing is the same as knowledge of knowing it. I say: this is false, because knowledge of a thing is a relation to the thing, and knowledge of knowledge of a thing is a relation between knowledge and knowledge of the thing, and the relation to the thing is different from the relation to something else. The answer: there is no end to the relations and attachments, and they are non-existential matters; what is actually established is knowledge, which is a single attribute, and in this there is the same objection as before. And what he answered regarding the third [objection] indicates his confusion, although he mentioned earlier that the truth is that knowledge is a relative matter, and here he makes it one thing with multiple relations, and he explicitly stated before that relations are non-existential, then when he became confused about it he favored the opinion of Abu Sahl by way of criticism.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit concerning divine knowledge. The objector argues that if God knows all things, then His knowledge would involve infinite regress: knowing that He knows, knowing that He knows that He knows, and so on. Since His objects of knowledge are infinite, this would generate infinite levels of knowledge, leading to an infinite number of attributes, which is impossible. Then Imam Razi presents a counter: someone might say that knowledge of a thing is identical to knowledge of knowing it. Razi refutes this by distinguishing relations: knowledge of a thing is a relation to the thing, while knowledge of knowing it is a relation between knowledge and itself or its act. These are different relations. The final answer is that the infinite regress occurs only in the relations and attachments (nisaab wa ta'allaqaat), which are non-existential (ghayr thubootiyya). What is actually existent is the attribute of knowledge itself, which is one. Relations are merely conceptual and do not multiply the attribute. However, Razi notes that this answer still faces the same objection (al-ishkal bi alladhi taqaddam). Then he adds a remark that his answer to the third objection shows his confusion, as he earlier affirmed that knowledge is relational and that relations are non-existential, but then he seems to waver and endorses Abu Sahl's view by way of criticism. This passage shows the complexity of the kalam discussion on divine attributes.

Key Terms
علم
معلومات
مراتب غير متناهية
نسب
تعلقات
غير ثبوتية
إضافة
أبو سهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's knowledge involves infinite relations, then the attribute of knowledge itself must be infinite or multiple.
Correction: Razi clarifies that relations are non-existential and do not affect the attribute itself; the attribute remains one.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the answer to the objection that God's knowledge leads to infinite attributes?
A) God does not know all things.
B) Knowledge is a single attribute, and the infinite regress occurs only in non-existential relations.
C) The objection is invalid because knowledge is identical to the act of knowing.
D) God's knowledge is limited.

Key Takeaway
The objection of infinite regress in divine knowledge is resolved by recognizing that relations are non-existential, so the attribute of knowledge remains single.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge encompasses every detail of your life, including your hidden thoughts and motives, does that change how you live today? Are you comfortable with being completely known? How does that affect your sincerity in worship and daily actions?
?
The objector thought infinite objects would multiply attributes, but Razi shows that relations are non-existential. Are there areas in your life where you multiply concerns about what others think, forgetting that only the essence of your character matters before God? How can you focus on the single attribute of sincerity?
?
Razi admits confusion about this topic and humbly favors another scholar's view. When you encounter a truth about Allah that you do not fully understand, do you stubbornly argue or accept with humility? What aspect of your faith requires you to trust in God's simplicity even when your mind struggles with complexity?

The Commentator's Critique of Infinite Non-Existential Relations
Commentator criticizes Razi's view on infinite non-existential relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's criticism of Imam Razi's view on infinite non-existential relations and appreciate the value of intellectual humility in theology.

Core Idea
True comprehension includes recognizing the limits of reason.

This passage presents a sharp critique of Imam Razi's attempt to reconcile infinite non-existential relations with the unity of divine knowledge. The commentator argues that such efforts lead to confusion, and that it is more honest and wise to acknowledge the incapacity of human intellect to fully grasp the divine essence and attributes. A famous maxim reinforces this: acknowledging inability is itself a form of understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the commentator's critical tone come through.
The passage uses technical terms like 'nisaab' (relations) and 'ghayr thubutiyya' (non-existential).
2
Identify Razi's two commitments
Find the two things Razi is said to have committed to: (1) the permissibility of infinite non-existential relations, and (2) making knowledge a single attribute despite the objection. Notice the commentator's disapproval.
The phrase 'fanzur fi tahayyurihi wa khabtihi' indicates confusion.
3
Understand the commentator's criticism
The commentator calls Razi's position confused and inconsistent. Ask yourself: why might holding both views at once be problematic?
The commentator implies that Razi's attempts to solve the problem lead to contradiction.
4
Reflect on the recommended alternative
The commentator suggests it would be better to admit that human intellect cannot reach the divine essence or ascertain the realities of His attributes. Why is this 'better'?
This is a call for epistemological humility in theology.
5
Analyze the maxim
The commentator states: 'The inability to grasp comprehension is comprehension.' What does this mean? How does it support the recommendation?
It implies that recognizing one's limitation is itself a form of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : التزم ههنا جواز كون النسب مع كونها غير ثبوتية غير متناهية ، وجعل في الأخير العلم العلم صفة واحدة مع التزام النقض عليه فانظر فى تحيره وخبطه فى هذا الموضع ، ولوقال عقول البشر لا تصل إلى اكتناه الذات ولا إلى تحقق حقائق صفاته لكان أولى ، فإن العجز عن درك الإدراك إدراك ، وتحقيق هذا المبحث يحتاج إلى كلام طويل لا يحتمله هذا الموضع .
I say: He committed here to the permissibility of relations being infinite while being non-existential, and in the end he made knowledge a single attribute while accepting the objection against it. So look at his confusion and inconsistency in this place. If he had said that human intellects cannot reach the essence (al-dhaat) nor ascertain the realities of His attributes, it would be better. For the inability to grasp comprehension is comprehension. And the verification of this topic requires lengthy speech that this place does not accommodate.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's critique of Imam Razi's handling of the infinite relations and the unity of divine knowledge. Razi admitted that relations (nisaab) can be infinite while being non-existential, and he maintained that knowledge is a single attribute, even though the objection (al-ishkal) still applies. The commentator finds this inconsistent and confused. He suggests a more humble approach: to admit that human intellect cannot fully comprehend the divine essence and attributes. He quotes a maxim: 'The inability to grasp comprehension is (itself) comprehension.' This implies that recognizing the limits of human reason is a kind of understanding. He also notes that a full resolution of this issue requires a long discussion not possible here.

Correction: The commentator suggests that intellectual humility is appropriate; acknowledging the limits of human intellect is itself a form of understanding.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator recommend instead of Razi's approach?
A) To provide a detailed proof of the unity of knowledge.
B) To admit that human intellect cannot fully comprehend the divine essence.
C) To deny that relations are non-existential.
D) To accept the infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The commentator criticizes Razi's attempt to harmonize infinite relations and unified divine knowledge, advocating instead for intellectual humility and recognizing the limits of human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith do you assume you must have full rational certainty, when in reality you might need to admit your limited comprehension? How does this affect your relationship with God?
?
The commentator says 'inability to grasp comprehension is comprehension.' How comfortable are you with not having all the answers about God? Can you think of a specific belief you struggle with because you can't fully understand it?
?
Are there times when your own intellectual pride—thinking you can fully grasp divine matters—has led you to confusion or inconsistency, similar to what the commentator attributed to Razi? What steps can you take to embrace intellectual humility?

Power Does Not Entail Effectiveness
Razi criticizes the inference from power to effectiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power over all possibles does not imply that He is effective in all of them.

Core Idea
Power alone does not produce effects; will is required.

Razi examines an argument that claims God's power over all possibles necessitates that nothing exists except by His power. He points out a critical flaw: power without will is insufficient for an effect. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the key terms: قادر (powerful) and مؤثر (effective).
2
Identify the inference
The original argument moves from God's power over all possibles to the conclusion that everything exists by His power. Find that inference in the text.
3
Understand Razi's objection
Razi says that power alone is not enough; will is also needed. What would happen if power alone sufficed?
He says: otherwise all possibles would exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى قوله إذا ثبت أنه قادر على جميع الممكنات وجب أن لا يوجد شيء من الممكنات إلا بقدرته ففيه نظر لأنه لا يلزم من كونه قادرا على جميع الممكنات كونه مؤثرا فى جميعها وإلا لزم منه وجود جميع الممكنات وذلك أن القدرة وحدها لا تكفى فى وجود التأثير بل يحتاج معها إلى الإرادة، والدليل الذي ذكره يدل على امتناع اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد ولم أيدل على امتناع اجتماع قادرين على مقدور واحد ، بل الصحيح عند أهل السنة أن الله تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات وغير مؤثر فى كلها والعبد قادر على البعض وغير مؤثر ، فهما إذا قادران على شيء واحد ، أن المؤثر فيه أحدهما دون الآخر ، وإنما كان ذلك كذلك لكون المؤثر محتا جامع القدرة مع إلى الإرادة والقادر هو الذى له القدرة فقط من حيث هو قادر ، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يمتنع أن يكون في ممكن مؤثر غير الله تعالى إلا أن بعض ذلك بغير ماذكره وفى عبارته عند قوله أو يقع بواحد منهما وهو محال لأن المانع من وقوعه بذاك فمالم يوجد وقوعه بهذا لا يمتنع وقوعه بذاك ، موضع نظر إذ كان من الواجب أن يقول فمالم يوجد وقوعه بهذا وجب وقوعه بذاك إذ ذلك مؤثر خال عن الموانع ، وباقى الكلام هكذا فلو لم يقع بهذا وذلك وقع بذلك وبهذا وهو محال .
And in his statement: When it is established that He is powerful over all possibles, it must be that nothing of the possibles exists except by His power, there is consideration in it. For it does not follow from His being powerful over all possibles that He is effective in all of them; otherwise it would necessitate the existence of all possibles, because power alone is insufficient for the occurrence of effect, rather will is needed with it. And the proof he mentioned indicates the impossibility of two agents meeting on one effect, but it does not indicate the impossibility of two powerful agents meeting on one object of power. Rather, according to Ahl al-Sunna, God is powerful over all possibles but not effective in all of them, and the servant is powerful over some but not effective, so they are two powerful agents over one thing, but the effective one is one of them, not the other. And that is so because the effect (al-mu'aththir) must combine power with will, while the powerful agent is the one who has power only insofar as he is powerful. On this basis, it is not impossible that there is in a possible an effective other than God, except that some of that is not as he mentioned. And in his statement when he said 'or it occurs by one of them, and it is impossible because the preventer of its occurrence by that prevents its occurrence by this, it is not impossible that it occurs by that' there is a point of consideration, for he should have said: 'as long as its occurrence by this has not occurred, it must occur by that, since that is an effective free from preventers.' And the rest of the speech is like this: 'So if it did not occur by this and that, it would occur by that and this, and it is impossible.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage critiques an argument that proceeds from God's power over all possibles to the conclusion that nothing exists except by His power. Razi points out several logical flaws. First, power alone does not entail influence (ta'thīr); will is also needed; otherwise all possibles would exist. Second, the argument correctly proves that two influences cannot meet on one effect, but it does not prove that two powerful agents cannot share an object of power. According to Sunni doctrine, God and the servant can both be powerful over the same possible, but only one is effective (the one with both power and will). Razi also criticizes the phrasing of the original argument: the condition for one agent to act is not merely the absence of prevention from the other; rather, if one does not act, the other (if effective) must act. He corrects the logical formulation.

Key Terms
قادر
مؤثر
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has power over something, He must bring it into existence.
Correction: Power alone does not entail existence; will is also necessary. God may have power over a possible but not will it.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does God's power over all possibles not imply that He is effective in all of them?
A) Because some possibles are evil and God would not do them.
B) Because power alone is not enough; will is also needed.
C) Because the servant's power limits God's effectiveness.
D) Because some possibles are impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
Power without will does not produce an effect; otherwise all possibles would exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to trust God's power, but do you ever find yourself anxious that His will might not align with your desires? How does this passage challenge you to submit to His will over your expectations of His power?
?
When you pray, do you assume that because God can answer, He must? How does this passage remind you that His will is distinct from His power, and call you to surrender to His wisdom?
?
Are there areas in your life where you act as if having the ability to do something means you should do it? How does this passage reframe that assumption and lead you to seek God's will before acting?
